==PAGE:3==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
III 
 
ABSTRACT  
In this thesis, a thorough selection of representative rain events from long-term historical rain series (10 years) 
has been conducted with the aim of increasing the computational efficiency related to estimation of Real Time 
Control (RTC) parameters. The hydraulic modelling was conducted based on a case study using a USEPA 
SWMM (Storm Water Management Model) model of the ‘Astlingen’ benchmark model, developed by the 
German Water Association (DWA). The estimation of RTC parameters was conducted using the Python 
package ‘PySWMM’, allowing interactions with the model, while using optimisation functions available in 
Python.  
Store units in the ‘Astlingen’ model have been divided into controllable and non-controllable units, based on 
their location in the urban drainage system (UDS). Estimates of relevant rain events potentially causing CSOs 
from the storage units, have been determined from the series. Two criteria were considered in the 
determination: For controllable storage units, no outflow from the storage units was considered (i.e. limited by 
control), thus any rainfall events exceeding the storage capacity was considered relevant. For the non-
controllable storage unit, constant outflow to the water treatment plant (WWTP), the relevance was determined 
based on rainfall events exceeding the storage capacity and the throttle outflow.  
The relevant event periods were extended (with 8 hours prior to the events and 24 hours after) to simulations 
periods. 647 simulation periods were identified, covering 44% of the total rain series. CSO occurs from 61% 
of the simulation periods, with an average of 338,000 m3 CSO volume per year. 
The year 2003 was found to be representative of the total rain series based on rainfall characteristics, quantity 
of simulation periods with CSOs, an estimation of rainfall events causing CSOs and a t-test between mean 
CSO volume determined for this year and all years. 
Based on the simulation periods for 2003, RTC parameters were estimated through optimisation, applying 
‘Nelder-Mead simplex algorithm’. All RTC applied involved control of orifices limiting the outflow of storage 
units based on the water level monitored by a sensor located in a downstream storage unit (feedback control 
loops). All reductions of CSO volumes and events achieved, have been compared to a defined Base Case (BC). 
The reductions of CSO volumes achieved based on rain events with depths corresponding to the 50-75 % 
largest events (depths of 7.4 – 10.6 mm), are considered most representative for the reductions achieved 
considering all events in the historical long-term rain series. Especially, The three simulation periods with the 
largest CSO volumes, were representative and used for any further estimation.  
The different RTC strategies showed similar results. The impacts of estimating RTC parameters based on 
objective functions considering potential sensitivities related to a CSOs discharge points (e.g. cost, 
environmental and humans risk,), or aiming to minimise the CSO frequency, have been investigated separately 
and combined within the strategies. Including the CSO frequency, results in larger CSO volumes but less 
events. However, the total CSO duration are longer when aiming to minimise the number of CSO events. 
Any RTC developed in this thesis, resulted in both increases and reductions of the CSO volumes and events at 
different locations in the system (storage units, CSO structures and manholes). This is in line with previous 
findings. Considering CSOs from the entire system, the RTC developed here result in larger reductions (24-31 
% for the CSO volumes and 7-32 % for events) than those found in literature using static control and Equal 
Filling Degree (22 % and 16 % reductions of the CSO volumes).

PREFACE  
 
This master thesis ‘Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, 
concentrating on selecting events from long-term historical rain series’ completes my master program in 
Environmental Engineering with specialisation in Urban Water at the Technical University of Denmark. The 
project was undertaken from 1st August 2019 to 1st February 2020. 
 
 
I would like to thank my supervisor Associate Professor Morten Borup for his support, guidance and 
suggestions during the months I have undertaken this project. I have appreciated your input and knowledge in 
the area. I would also like to thank Congcong Sun for letting me use her SWMM version of the ‘Astlingen’ 
Benchmark model and Magnus Bjerrum Johansen for great help and guidance regarding SWMM and 
PySWMM.  
 
A special thanks to Christiane, Camilla and Alexandre for great friendships and teamwork throughout our 
degree, without you it would not have been as enjoyable as it has been.  
 
Last, but not least, I want to thank my family, friends and fiancé, Anders, for moral support and encouragement 
throughout my degree. I could not have done this without any of you - especially not without constant support 
from my sister Kristine.

Figure 1:  Storage volume as a function of rainfall event duration (Modified version of figure in Butler and 
Davies 2011) ...................................................................................................................................................... 5 
Figure 2: Flow diagram of RTC (Zacharof, Schütze, and Butler 2003). .......................................................... 6 
Figure 3: An example of a feedback control loop. The sensor is located downstream of the controllable 
actuator (e.g. an orifice). When the water level in the manhole (the controlled variable) measured by the sensor 
deviates from the set point, the orifice will close, and the water will be stored in the storage unit. ................. 7 
Figure 4: An example of a feed forward control loop. The orifice will close, and the water will be stored in 
the storage unit, when the water level in the storage unit (the controlled variable) measured by the sensor, 
deviates from the set point. It is predetermined how much water can be in the storage unit before the manhole 
floods.  A model with a certain accuracy is needed to predict how much the water in the storage unit 
corresponds to the water level in the manhole. ................................................................................................. 7 
Figure 5: The overland flow (runoff) on a sub-catchment is determined using a nonlinear reservoir-model 
(Rossman and Huber 2016). .............................................................................................................................. 9 
Figure 6: Orifices limits the flow downstream by decreasing the inlet area to the downstream node or storage 
unit. The shape of orifices can be circular or rectangular. Modified version of figure in (Butler and Davies 
2011). ............................................................................................................................................................... 11 
Figure 7: A conceptual model of the Astlingen benchmark model. Provided by (Schütze et al. 2017). ........ 13 
Figure 8: The catchment of the ‘Astlingen’ benchmark model in SWMM. ................................................... 14 
Figure 9: For Tank 4, the total depth of a rainfall event must exceed 4.29 mm within 24 hours to be considered 
relevant (𝑖= 124 ℎ𝑃𝑖 > 4.29 mm). The start of the rainfall event exceeding the criteria is located along with 
the end of the event. For the simulation period of the relevant events, the event period is extended with 8 hours 
before the event starts and a ‘dry’ period of 24 hours after the event ends (≤ 1 mm for 24 hours). The solid line 
after the rainfall event end is 24 hours, but due to the smaller event occurring after the end of the rainfall event 
exceeding the criteria, the dry period criteria is not reached 24 hours after the event end. A dry period between 
the two identified events are excluded from the figure. .................................................................................. 22

unit but is included when eliminating overlaps. .............................................................................................. 23

Figure 23: Interactions and actions performed for the optimisation of an objective function. Based on the set 
point (an estimated RTC parameter), control actions are defined in Python (e.g. Orifice V4 must close when 
the water level in Tank 1 exceeds 1 meter).  PySWMM interacts with SWMM, thus a simulation is conducted. 
When the sensor located in the urban drainage system monitored the set point, the controller will regulate the 
actuator according to the control action. Based on the results of the simulation, the result of the objective 
function will be determined and evaluated. Depending on the evaluation, a new set point is determined by the 
optimiser, thus new control actions are defined. This continues until the minimum result of the objective 
function is reached. .......................................................................................................................................... 45 
Figure 24: The simulation periods defined for year 2003 is divided into six groups based on the maximum 
rainfall depth occuring 30 minutes prior to an CSO occurrence in the simulation periods. ............................ 48

1 INTRODUCTION  
The reduction of pervious areas in urban areas along with climate changes, leads to increased risk of flooding 
of cities and combined sewer overflows (CSO). The population growth in cities requires construction of roads 
and buildings, leading to more impermeable areas in cities (García et al. 2015). Climate changes in North 
Europe involves an increase in the annual precipitation and higher frequency of extreme weather. An increase 
of up to 30% in the magnitude of precipitation in the winter is expected in North-East Europe as a result of a 
change in the annual precipitation pattern (European Commission 2009; European Environment Agency 
2017). Extreme rain events with higher intensities, frequencies and durations are expected (European 
Commission 2017, 2009; García et al. 2015). The additional rain- and stormwater to be handled in the cities 
strains the existing urban drainage systems (European Commission 2009).  
Functional urban drainage systems collecting and transporting wastewater and stormwater out of cities are 
crucial in the matter of reducing costs and health risks related to damages from overflow and flooding of 
systems and cities (García et al. 2015; Lund et al. 2018). For an existing urban drainage system, there are 
different alternatives to cope with the challenges. Adding more pipes or expanding the pipes or storage units 
of an existing system is costly and might not be feasible in established cities (van Daal et al. 2017). Stormwater 
source control (SC) approaches has the purpose of delaying or reducing the runoff volume. The implementation 
costs depend on the specific case and the application of CS can be applied as an effective supplement to other 
management controls of the system (García et al. 2015). An alternative, more cost-efficient solution requiring 
none or minimal expansion of the existing system, is the efficient Real Time Control (RTC) of existing urban 
drainage systems. Through dynamic precipitation patterns, static or dynamic control algorithms and hydraulic 
modelling, it is possible to better utilise the available capacity of the pipes, manholes and storage basins 
constituting an urban drainage system (García et al. 2015; Löwe et al. 2016). Besides reducing flood of cities 
and minimise CSOs in urban drainage systems, improving the performance of the system with RTC bring other 
advantages, including a reduction in overload of wastewater treatment plants, resulting in an optimised 
treatment and improved water quality of the treated water and the environment, and the operational costs 
related to the treatment as well as the urban drainage system (Vezzaro et al. 2014; Garofalo et al. 2017; Lund 
et al. 2018).   
Simulations conducted based on long-term rain series account for parameters not included for simulations 
conducted based on single rain events. Important parameters impacting evaporation, infiltration and the current 
state of the urban drainage system are automatically accounted for using long-term rain series (Gironas, 
Roesner, and Davis 2009). The main disadvantage of simulating based on long-term rain series, is the 
increasing computation time required for successful simulations (Rossman and Huber 2016). 
Most of the research available regarding developing of the best RTC parameters are limited by case-specific 
frameworks, hence compromising the application and comparison of these results with other sites (Schütze et 
al. 2017). This perception is shared with (Kroll et al. 2018), which highlights the benefits of using benchmark 
models to raise awareness of RTC, but simultaneously emphasizes the importance of applying case-specific 
developed controls elsewhere with great care. The research conducted regarding RTC, varies widely from 
investigations of increasing the RTC potential (Zacharof, Schütze, and Butler 2003), to simplification of the 
hydraulic models used for the estimation of RTC parameters (Kroll 2019), or studies of the effort and benefits 
related to developing control actions based on forecasting or using simple control rules (Lund et al. 2018; 
Hutton et al. 2014). Studies has found simple rules (e.g. Rule Based Control) to be considered equally sufficient 
as models based on forecasting (e.g. Model Predictive Control), particular for smaller catchments, whereas 
larger networks were found benefit more from advanced control (Kroll 2019). Despite of the many studies 
available regarding RTC, highlighting the benefits of introducing RTC. (Kroll et al. 2018) suggests that the 
lack of documentation regarding application of control concepts on a wide range of cases, is one of the reasons 
for the continuous reservation existing among wastewater operators for introducing RTC. 
==PAGE:15==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 2 of 88 
 
 
Aiming to explain the core principles and methods of RTC to interested wastewater operators, the working 
group ‘Integral Real Time Control’ of the German Water Association (DWA) has developed a benchmark 
model named ’Astlingen’ (Schütze et al. 2017). Defining benchmark models to simplify and improving the 
application and development of a strategies to control wastewater, is not a new thing. An established 
benchmark model (The ‘Benchmark Simulation Model’ (IWA Benchmark plant, Gernaey et al. 2014)) is 
frequently used by the water treatment and control community in the development and improvement of control 
strategies for wastewater treatment plants (WWTPs).  
 
1.1 Thesis statement 
The purpose of introducing Real Time Control (RTC), is the reducing Combined Sewer Overflow (CSOs) and 
preventing flooding of cities through better utilisation of the capacity of the system. The aim of this master 
thesis is to investigate and develop a method significantly increasing the computational efficiency related to 
the estimation of RTC parameters. Concentrating on selecting important rainfall events from long-term rain 
series and simple optimisation of RTC parameters. The secondary aim is therefore to determine the minimum 
quantity of rainfall events needed to achieve reductions of CSO volumes and events, representative for the 
historical long-term rain series. With the objective of developing method easy applicable for others to use in 
the estimation of RTC parameters independent on the urban drainage system it is applied for, the method will 
be based on general assumptions.  
The investigations will be case specific and the results of introducing and optimising RTC parameters will be 
evaluated based on the reduction in CSO volume and frequency. The case specific study will involve hydraulic 
modelling of the benchmark model ‘Astlingen’, a hypothetical combined and separate sewer system developed 
by the working group ‘Integral Real Time Control’ of the German Water Association (DWA). The hydraulic 
modelling involves executing simulations using the United States Environmental Protection Agency’s Storm 
Water Management Model (SWMM) through Python using the PySWMM package, along with additional 
mathematic optimisation and programming. 
 
1.2 Methodology  
The following sections are divided into four parts, in addition to limitations/recommendations for further work 
and a conclusion: Theory, Case study: ‘Astlingen’ benchmark model, Selecting rainfall events for the 
estimation of control parameters and Estimating control parameters.  
The aim of the theory section is to provide information on the content referred to in later sections. The section 
contains descriptions of the constructions of urban drainage systems, Real Time Control - application as well 
as the related benefits and challenges, and the modelling compartments runoff and routing constituting the 
software Storm Water Management Model (SWMM) used for the hydraulic modelling. Lastly, section contains 
a short explanation of the theory behind a t-test conducted in a later section (‘Selecting rainfall events for the 
estimation of control parameters’). 
The section ‘Case study: ‘Astlingen’ benchmark model’ contains a description of hydraulic characteristics of 
the Base Case used in the analyses conducted and discussed in following sections. Furthermore, the section 
contains a description of the hydraulic model in SWMM and modifications made of the original model 
provided. 
There are two subsections in the ‘Selecting rainfall events for the estimation of control parameters’-section, 
the methodology used in the section and the results and discussions related to the selection of important rainfall 
events. The rationale of using long-term rain series prior to CDS rains is described, along with the criteria 
made to determine the relevance of rainfall events. Additionally, identification of simulation periods and

analyses of the rainfall events within the simulation periods are conducted and discussed with the aim of

2 THEORY  
2.1 Urban drainage system 
Urban drainage systems have the purpose of collecting, storing and conveying wastewater and stormwater in 
developed urban areas. Therefore, urban drainage systems are crucial to retain a high life standard, by 
minimising environmental, economic and health risks related to flooding. Wastewater is highly polluted used 
water, original supplied to obtain domestic, institutional or industrial purposes. Wastewater has a daily 
variation and is considered more regular than stormwater. Stormwater is defined as rainfall falling on 
impermeable catchment surfaces such as impervious roofs and paved areas. Stormwater is less polluted than 
wastewater but is considered polluted due to wash off of pollutants from the surfaces. (Butler and Davies 2011; 
Winther et al. 2016). 
The entire infrastructural system consists of several components. Rainfall falling on permeable surfaces 
evaporate or infiltrates into the ground. Wastewater and stormwater from roofs flows from the individual 
properties to sewers through drains, while runoff from impermeable surfaces enters the sewers through gullies 
located at low points, typically along road channels. The collected stormwater and wastewater can be conveyed 
from an urban area to a recipient based on two main principles: combined sewer system conveying all water 
in a single pipe or separate sewer system transporting wastewater and stormwater in independent pipes, or a 
combination of the two (Butler and Davies 2011) The sewers can be inspected through manholes, which size 
depends on the connected pipes. Furthermore, the sewers often contain storage units such as detention basins 
storing or combined sewer overflow (CSO) structures (Butler and Davies 2011; Winther et al. 2016).  
2.1.1 Combined sewer system 
Combined sewer systems are predominant in Europe and North America (Lund et al. 2018). In a combined 
system, wastewater and stormwater is transported through a single pipe to a wastewater treatment plant 
(WWTP), before the treated water is discharged to the environment at a watercourse (Lund et al. 2018). During 
dry weather, wastewater will be the only water type constituting the flow in a combined system, whereas the 
additional stormwater will be the dominant type during small but more frequent wet weather events. During 
larger and less frequent rain events, stormwater might compose fifty to one hundred times the average 
wastewater flow in the combined sewer system (Butler and Davies 2011). Due to dry periods occurring more 
frequent than rainy periods and to economic reasons, the capacity of the sewer systems and WWTPs are 
designed to handle small events (Butler and Davies 2011; Lund et al. 2018). Therefore, a greater contact 
between the liquid and pipe wall occurs during dry periods, where the flowrate is relatively low, resulting in 
risk of sediment deposition (Butler and Davies 2011).  
 During heavy rainfall (rain events with high magnitude and low frequency), the high flow-rates in a combined 
system might exceed the inlet capacity of WWTPs, causing CSOs (Garofalo et al. 2017). If storage units are 
implemented in the sewer system, water can be temporary stored until being further discharged to either a 
WWTP or as CSO, depending on the capacity of the system. In order to protect the system against hydraulic 
overload, CSO structures are implemented in the system to release a part of the untreated wastewater and 
stormwater out of the system, through a CSO structure and into a receiving watercourse, such as rivers or lakes. 
The most frequent CSO structure is a weir, where the magnitude of the CSO depends on the crest height of the 
weir. A too low crest height results in polluted water frequently being discharged to the watercourse, whereas 
a too high crest height might result in risk of flooding due to surcharge of the upstream part of the system. The 
CSO structures should be designed with the aim of relieving the system during peak periods by discharging 
polluted water when water level increases, but without decreasing the downstream flow to the WWTP. To 
minimise the pollution of the watercourse, CSO structures are designed to withhold as much solids in the 
system as possible. (Butler and Davies 2011; Ahm et al. 2016). CSOs are considered a major contributor to 
water pollution, despite of CSOs being diluted by the present water in the receiving water bodies, as well as 
inflow-infiltration can have a dilutive effect. (Butler and Davies 2011; Garofalo et al. 2017).  
==PAGE:18==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 5 of 88 
 
 
Furthermore, heavy rain and inflow-infiltration in combined as well as separate systems, might lead to local 
flooding of basements and CSOs of manholes causing untreated water onto streets (Garofalo et al. 2017; Lund 
et al. 2018). Flooding and CSOs poses a risk to the environment especially as the beginning of an event, where 
the pollution is concentrated due to the first flush (e.g. catchment washoff or pipe sediment), as well as it poses 
a risk for humans (Butler and Davies 2011).  
2.1.2 Separate sewer system  
In separate sewer system, wastewater and stormwater is collected and transported in independent pipes, hence 
this type of sewer systems is more costly (Lund et al. 2018).  Wastewater flows variates throughout the day. 
Therefore, wastewater pipes are dimensioned to transport the maximum daily flow to the WWTP, without 
causing overflows (Butler and Davies 2011). Wastewater pipes are significantly smaller than pipes in a 
combined system and stormwater pipes. Stormwater pipes are designed to collect the stormwater and discharge 
it directly into the watercourse. CSOs are eliminated in separate systems as no untreated wastewater is 
intentionally discharged to the watercourse (Stauffer and Spuhler 2019). To minimise pollution of the receiving 
watercourse, stormwater pipes are often situated on top of wastewater pipes or side-by-side (Butler and Davies 
2011; Winther et al. 2016). Stormwater might enter the wastewater pipe through inflow-infiltration, hence the 
limited capacity in the wastewater pipe can be challenged (Butler and Davies 2011).    
2.1.3 Dimensioning storage units 
The hydraulic considerations for the capacity of storage units involves the size and outflow rate of the storage 
unit. The maximum outflow rate might a fixed value, often limited by the capacity of the downstream system 
or receiving watercourse. The size of a storage unit depends on the rainfall return period it is designed to handle 
without causing CSOs. Using a constant outflow rate, the storage volume can be determined as a function of 
the event durations of a specific return period. Using this methodology, the storage volume is determined based 
on the event duration being most critical for the storage unit. The methodology is shown on Figure 1, where 
the inflow volume (Vin) for different event durations (D) is shown along the corresponding CSO volume (Vout), 
using a constant outflow rate. A storage unit will be filled with water when the inflow rate exceeds the outflow 
rate, whereas the storage empties when the outflow rate exceeds the inflow rate. Therefore, the difference 
between the two volumes represents the storage volume required at different durations (S), hence the largest 
difference is the maximum storage volume required (Smax). This point is considered the critical point and is 
used as the design volume of the storage unit (Smax) (Butler and Davies 2011). 
 
 
Figure 1:  Storage volume as a function of rainfall event duration (Modified version of figure in Butler and Davies 2011) 
==PAGE:19==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 6 of 88 
 
 
2.2 Real Time Control (RTC) 
Climate changes and increasing urbanisation strains the already challenged urban drainage system. A cost-
efficient method to mitigate to the challenges, while reducing CSOs and flooding from urban drainage systems, 
is through control of the existing system (García et al. 2015; Garofalo et al. 2017).  Control of an urban drainage 
system can be used as an alternative or in addition to more traditional management approaches, such as 
expansion or separation of sewer systems (Vitasovic 2006; Pabst 2019).  
Urban drainage systems can be controlled with passive control, where static not-adjustable hydraulic elements 
such as gates, weirs and valves are controlled by fixed settings, or with active Real Time Control (RTC) (Lund 
et al. 2018). Based on continuously monitored measurements of the current state of the system, control actions 
are determined according to Real Time Control algorithms. Hereby, the state of the system is adjusted by 
dynamically operating the system to maintain and achieve operational objectives and limit the occurrences of 
unwanted effects (Butler and Davies 2011; Leitão et al. 2017; Lund et al. 2018)  
RTC is a case-dependent technology used to manage urban drainage systems, as there is not identified a single 
solution that ensures a successful design and implementation of RTC of all urban drainage systems  (Kroll et 
al. 2018).  
Control of an urban drainage system are often induced to achieve objectives, often imposed by regulations or 
guidelines. A case-specific control strategy is made to meet the objectives, through control algorithms, Figure 
2 (Zacharof, Schütze, and Butler 2003).  
 
 
Figure 2: Flow diagram of RTC (Zacharof, Schütze, and Butler 2003). 
The potential, benefits and goal of applying RTC should be defined prior to implementation to minimise the 
related costs (García et al. 2015; van Daal et al. 2017).  
2.2.1 Elements/equipment   
RTC systems is constituted by several elements. A sensor monitors the current state of the system. When the 
controlled variable reaches a certain predefined set-point, the controller adjusts the actuator (Lund et al. 2018).  
 
Sensors continuously monitors the current state of the system. There are several sensor types suitable for 
continuous recording a controlled variable, such as flow gauges, rain gauges, water level gauges or water 
quality gauges. Water level gauges is the most commonly used, because it is particular suitable for projecting 
the state in the storage unit or converting waters depths to flow rates (Butler and Davies 2011; Pabst 2019).  
 
Controllers receives signal from the sensors. Based on a comparison between the current state of the system 
and the operational objectives, the controllers adjust the actuators according to the predefined control 
algorithms, hence a controller is needed for every actuator. There are two widely used types of controller 
software: discrete and continuous  (Butler and Davies 2011; Lund et al. 2018; Pabst 2019): 
 
==PAGE:20==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 7 of 88 
 
 
- 
The two-point discrete controller switches between on/off or open/close, often used for pumps. It 
activates the actuator when the sensor measures a predefined set point and stop at another predefined 
set point. The disadvantages include high frequency of switching.  
 
- 
The three-point discrete controller functions the same way as two-point, but with a dead ban (gap) 
between the two set points, where nothing happens. Using this controller, the disadvantage of 
excessive switching frequency is reduced. This type is mainly used for orifices and weirs.  
 
- 
The proportional-integral-derivative (PID) controller is a commonly used continuous controller, 
changing the settings of the actuator continuously.  
 
Actuators are controllable devices used to control e.g. water flows in an urban drainage system. Based on the 
signal from the controllers, the actuators changes the settings of the dynamic controllable elements, such as 
movable orifices or pumps (Butler and Davies 2011; Lund et al. 2018).  
 
The set point is the point at which the actuator changes position. The data transmission systems, transmit the 
collected data from the sensors to the controllers, and signals from controllers to actuators (Pabst 2019).  
 
2.2.2 Control loop  
The sensors, controllers, actuators and data transmission systems form a control loop. The controlled variable 
measured by the sensor, is compared to the predefined set point. The controller adjusts the actuator based on 
the comparison performed in the control loop (Zacharof, Schütze, and Butler 2003). The control loop can be 
formed as a feedback control or a feed forward control.  
 
In a feedback control loop (Figure 3) the sensor is located downstream of the actuator. The controlled variable 
is feedback and compared to the predefined set point. The difference between the set point and the controlled 
variable is referred to as the error term (e). Based on the error term, a controller is used to generate suitable 
actuator commands minimising the error term, with the aim of the error term driving to zero over time (Butler 
and Davies 2011; Garofalo et al. 2017).   
  
 
 
 
 
The senor in feed forward control loops (Figure 4) is located upstream of the actuator. A model is used to 
predetermine the deviation of the control variable from the set point. The actuator is activated in advance to 
compensate. The accuracy depends on the accuracy of the model predetermination (Butler and Davies 2011).

unit

(Actuator)

Figure 3: An example of a feedback control loop. The sensor is located downstream of the controllable actuator (e.g. an orifice). When
the water level in the manhole (the controlled variable) measured by the sensor deviates from the set point, the orifice will close, and
the water will be stored in the storage unit. 
Figure 4: An example of a feed forward control loop. The orifice will close, and the water will be stored in the storage unit, when 
the water level in the storage unit (the controlled variable) measured by the sensor, deviates from the set point. It is predetermined 
how much water can be in the storage unit before the manhole floods.  A model with a certain accuracy is needed to predict how 
much the water in the storage unit corresponds to the water level in the manhole.

Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 8 of 88 
 
 
2.2.3 RTC potential  
Screening of a system to evaluate the RTC potential is considered a useful tool to simplify the optimisation of 
an urban drainage system (Kroll et al. 2018). There is several appropriate screening tools to determine the RTC 
potential of a system (García et al. 2015).  Conventionally, the RTC potential of a system was evaluated based 
on a detailed study of the system (Zacharof, Schütze, and Butler 2003). In the literature, larger catchments are 
found to have larger RTC potential, thus controlling a system with RTC is found most beneficial for larger 
catchments (Kroll et al. 2018). 
For hydraulic under- as well as over-designed urban drainage systems, the benefits and effects of using real 
time control of a system will be small. Systems with small storage capacities will cause lack of storage units 
to retain water, whereas flooding and CSOs is not expected to occur frequent from systems with over-
dimensioned storage capacities, hence control is not considered beneficial nor desired. For urban drainage 
systems with more proper design properties, the control can be used to better utilize the system, and thereby 
make use of the potential to optimise the performance of the system (Butler and Davies 2011). 
RTC are most efficient and suitable for smaller events causing flooding or CSOs in the passive urban drainage 
system. The return periods of these smaller events depend solely on the hydraulic design dimensions of the 
given for the system. During extreme events the capacity of most urban drainage systems will be exceeded 
hence better utilization of the system is not considered relevant. However, for extreme events, RTC can be 
used to provide a more evenly distribution of CSOs or locate flooding to more suitable locations, e.g. green 
areas (Butler and Davies 2011).  
2.2.4 RTC strategy and algorithms 
The operational objectives of an urban drainage system can be achieved through control algorithms. The 
algorithms might contain more than one objective, for different times or during dry or wet weather periods 
(Vitasovic 2006).  
Flow controls includes limiting the inflow to an urban drainage system or the inflow/outflow rate from 
elements in the system, with the purpose of reducing CSOs or local flooding through better utilisation of the 
system (Butler and Davies 2011) 
The control algorithms can be local, global or integrated. For local control systems, actuators are adjusted 
based on data collected directly on the actuator site, e.g. controlling a actuator according to requirements of a 
related downstream WWTP or opening an movable orifice based on a monitored water level in related storage 
unit (Butler and Davies 2011; Kroll et al. 2018). For globally controlled actuators, system, the actuators are 
adjusted taking the entire system into account, e.g. the water volumes in upstream and downstream storages is 
linked with the purpose of avoiding emptying the upstream storage into an already full downstream tank. 
Lastly, for the not widely used integrated control system, actuators are adjusted and influenced by process 
measurements derived from systems outside the sewer system, e.g. a WWTP or a river (Butler and Davies 
2011).  
The control algorithms can be developed offline or online. For online developed control algorithms, a model 
is used to continuously optimising the settings of the actuators in the system, based on an evaluation of the 
current state of the system. The settings found most beneficial for every control step is applied to the model. 
An optimisation function can be used in the development, along with a prediction of the future state of the 
system (Vitasovic 2006; Leitão et al. 2017; Pabst 2019).  
Off-line algorithms are fixed control rules developed through iteration, using expert knowledge and the trial-
and-error approach. Rules are tested and evaluated until a sufficient result is obtained, and the rules can be 
applied to the system. There can be applied a set of rules for each scenario (Butler and Davies 2011; Leitão et 
al. 2017; Pabst 2019).  
==PAGE:22==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 9 of 88 
 
 
Rule-based control (RBC) is considered one of the simplest and most applied off-line control algorithms. RBC 
includes static IF-THEN controls, as both two- and three-point setups, and decision matrices (Pabst 2019). The 
‘IF’-part refers to the current state of the simulated system, and the ‘THEN’-part is the corresponding action 
occurring as a consequence of the current state. Decision matrices contain all possible inputs and their 
corresponding control actions (García et al. 2015).  
The control algorithms can be based on different principles. Some general algorithms are preferential upstream 
storage, aiming to retain water in the upper part of the system to reduce flooding downstream of the system, 
or preferential downstream storage with the purpose of retaining water downstream to reduce upstream CSOs. 
(Butler and Davies 2011).  
Another, simple but widely used strategy is the Equal Filling Degree (EFD), aiming to balance the storage in 
the system at any time by equally distributing the water (Butler and Davies 2011; Dirckx, Assel, and Weemaes 
2014). At any predefined time step, the filling degrees of the storage units are compared to the average filling 
degree of the entire urban drainage system (Dirckx et al. 2011). The filling degree of a storage unit is 
determined by converting the monitored water levels of the activated storages into static storage volumes and 
dividing this with the maximum static storage volume available before CSOs (Dirckx et al. 2011). The storage 
can be a storage unit (e.g. basin) or an in-sewer storage, by which the maximum storage volume refers to the 
crest height of the storage unit or the manhole ground level (Kroll et al. 2018).  Based on the comparison, the 
actuator will change settings (e.g. open/close) (Dirckx, Assel, and Weemaes 2014). 
2.3 Storm Water Management Model (SWMM) 
Storm Water Management Model (SWMM) is a dynamic open-source rainfall-runoff simulation model 
developed by the United States Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) (Rossman 2015). The simulation tool 
can be used to evaluate quantity and quality of stormwater runoff and urban drainage systems (combined as 
well as separate) in relation to planning, analysing and design of sewer networks in urban areas (Rossman 
2015; Riaño-Briceño et al. 2016). The model consists of two modelling components: runoff and routing  
(Rossman 2015).  
2.3.1 Runoff model 
The runoff component generates runoff and pollutant loads based on the precipitation (single events or long-
term rain series) assigned as input to the sub-catchment through the rain gage. The overland runoff generated 
from the sub-catchments are determined using a nonlinear reservoir-model, taken the input of precipitation 
(rainfall and snowmelt) and the potential losses of evaporation (constant or time depended) and infiltration 
into account (Rossman and Huber 2016), see Figure 5. 
 
Figure 5: The overland flow (runoff) on a sub-catchment is determined using a nonlinear reservoir-model (Rossman and Huber 2016). 
The catchment is divided into sub-catchment considered as a rectangular surface with a width (W) and a 
uniform slope (S),  properties such as the perviousness or imperviousness, for both with or without depression, 
describing the infiltration and storage potential of the sub-catchment, and the outlet point from which runoff 
is discharged to other sub-catchments or the urban drainage system. The ponded water must exceed the depth 
==PAGE:23==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 10 of 88 
 
 
of the depression storages (ds) for impervious and pervious surfaces prior for runoff to occur, whereas 
impervious areas without depressions immediately contributes with runoff, hence representing areas connected 
to a drainage system. Depression storages takes initial rainfall abstractions, such as surface ponding and 
wetting, and interceptions of water absorbing elements into account (Rossman 2015).  
Different models can be chosen to determine the infiltration rate into the upper part of the unsaturated zone, 
including the empirical Horton’s infiltration equation, stating that the infiltration decreases exponential from 
the initial state (e.g. the beginning of an event) towards a steady-state, using a decay coefficient of the decrease 
rate and a time coefficient (Rossman 2015).   
The sub-catchment runoff is determined using the following differential equation, of the mass balance of the 
difference between the inflow and outflow rates (
∂d
dt) of the sub-catchment (Rossman and Huber 2016). 
 
∂d
dt = P −E −I −α ∙(d −ds)5/3 
Equation 1 
With P being the precipitation rate [m/s], E the evaporation rate [m/s], I the infiltration rate [m/s], d −ds is 
the water ponding on the surface of the sub-catchment [m] and α is Manning’s equation.  
α is the determined from the following Equation 2 (Rossman and Huber 2016). 
 
α = 1.49 ∙W ∙S1/2
A ∙n
 
Equation 2 
With W [m] and S [-] being the width and bed slope of the sub-catchment, respectively. A is the surface area 
of the sub-catchment [m2] and n is Manning’s number of the surface resistance [m1/3/s].  
 
2.3.2 Routing model 
The routing model simulates the flows within the sewer network. The inflow to the network the overland flow 
simulated by the runoff model and external flows including the dry weather flows (DWF) or inflow-infiltration, 
potentially assigned to specific entry points in the system. The sewer networks can consist of nodes, pipes, 
storage units, pumps, flow dividers and control devises (e.g. flow controllers such as weirs or orifices). CSO 
structures are simulated as nodes.  As an option, treatment can be applied to nodes, storage units etc. The 
routing component models the flows in the system, including convey of water within the network, surcharge 
or surface ponding. Furthermore, the system can be controlled by user-defined actions through pumps, static 
or dynamic controllers (Rossman 2015). 
2.3.2.1 
Flow routing 
The flows in the sewer network varies over long distances, hence the friction and slope cannot be considered 
insignificant. Based on the simulated lateral inflow to the sewer network, the unsteady flows in the sewers are 
simulated using the one-dimensional Saint-Venant equations (SVE). SVE consists of two equations, the 
dynamic equation representing the momentum – the variation of velocity in time, due to external forces – and 
a continuity equation representing the mass conservation – the variation of the total fluid mass depending on 
inflow and outflow of water (Zacharof, Schütze, and Butler 2003; Rossman 2015).  
The SVE equations can be solved using simplified the steady flow routing method, kinematic wave routing or 
dynamic wave routing. All three methods use Manning equation to convert flow rates to flow depths and 
slopes. Using the most complex routing method, dynamic wave routing, backwater e.g. due to control, 
surcharging from (pressurized) pipes due flows exceeding the flow of fully running pipes, and flooding from 
nodes caused by exceedance of the maximum depth available, can be simulated. This flow routing method 
requires small time steps to obtain accuracy causing longer computation time. If chosen, SWMM automatically 
reduce the predefined timestep resolution (Rossman 2015).  
==PAGE:24==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 11 of 88 
 
 
2.3.3 Nodes, pipes and storage units 
In the model used for the case study described in section 3 ‘CASE: ‘ASTLINGEN’’, nodes are assigned to 
join links, hence they represent manholes, CSOs, etc. For each node, an invert level and a maximum depth to 
store water representing the height difference between the ground and invert level,  must be assigned, whereas 
water surcharging in the nodes due to lack of capacity can be considered lost or allowed to pond on top of the 
node until available capacity of the system allows the water to drain back, or be considered lost. For the pipes, 
parameters affecting the flow must be assigned, including connection points (inlet and outlet), cross section 
shapes and related parameters, roughness, volume and potential flap gate preventing reverse flows. The flow 
(Q) in circular pipes are determined using the following Equation 3 (Rossman 2015).  
 
𝑄= 1.49

With n being the Manning roughness coefficient describing the roughness of the pipe surface [m1/3/s], A is the 
cross-sectional area [m2], R the hydraulic radius [m], and S is the pipe slope [-].  
As for nodes, storage units must be assigned an invert level and a maximum depth, along with a volumetric 
description of the size and shape, potential inflow and the outflow node (Rossman 2015). The outflow from a 
storage unit is determined as a function of the water level within the storage unit (Rossman 2017).  
2.3.4 Flow regulators 
Available flow regulators to be applied in a SWMM model includes static (weirs and outlets) and dynamic 
(orifices and gates) controllers. Orifices limits the flow downstream by forcing the flow through an opening 
with a, potentially adjustable, smaller area than the downstream element (pipe or storage unit). The orifice can 
be either circular, decreasing the flow by containing a smaller circular, or rectangular, covering the upper part 
of the downstream inlet area (Butler and Davies 2011), see Figure 6. Therefore, orifices are typically 
implemented at the inlet of manholes or storage units in SWMM, represented as a link between two nodes (e.g. 
with one of them being a storage unit)(Rossman 2015). Orifices can be located at the bottom or side of the 
upstream element (node or storage unit). Since the opening of the orifices might be controllable, the maximum 
opening height must be defined in SWMM, along with the discharge coefficient (Rossman 2015).  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The maximum flow (Qmax) through an orifice simulated in SWMM is determined based on the Bernoulli 
equation and a orifice discharge coefficient, by the following Equation 4 (Butler and Davies 2011; Rossman 
2015). 
 
Qmax = C ∙A ∙√2 ∙g ∙H 
Equation 4 
Where C is the unitless orifice discharge coefficient included in the equation for accuracy, A is the cross-
sectional area of the orifice [m2], g is the gravitational acceleration constant [m/s2] and H is the head difference 
across the orifice [m].

2.4 Statistics (t-test)  
A two-sample independent (Welch) t-test can be used to investigate whether a selected sample from a 
population can be considered representative the entire population. A null hypothesis (H0) and an alternative 
hypothesis (HA) is formulated. The null hypothesis is the mean values of the two independent groups are 
statistically equal, hence follows the same distribution, whereas the alternative states a statistical difference in 
the mean values, Equation 6.  
 
H0: μ1 = μ2  
HA: μ1 ≠ μ2   
Equation 6 
 
Using a predefined significant level (α), the null hypothesis is either rejected or accepted. The two groups 
must be approximately normal distributed and independent. For sample sizes of different sizes and unequal 
variances, the t-value can are determined using Equation 7 (Bacher 2019).

i. A large t-value indicates that the means of the investigated groups are different. For two normally distributed 
samples or samples with a large number of observations within the samples and unequal variances, a t-
distribution is assumed with the following the degrees of freedom Equation 8 (Bacher 2019).

3 CASE: ‘ASTLINGEN’  
3.1 The ‘Astlingen’ benchmark network 
The Astlingen worked example contains a hypothetical combined and separate sewer system meeting the 
German design guidelines and is considered representable for typical Central European conditions. The 
comprehensive worked example ‘Astlingen’ is developed by the working group ‘Integral Real Time Control’ 
of the German Water Association (DWA), aiming to explain the core principles and methods of RTC to 
interested wastewater operators. The benchmark model is developed with aim of making it easy applicable to 
develop, evaluate and compare RTC strategies of any desired system, on the thoroughly tested Astlingen 
example (Schütze et al. 2017).  
The benchmark model consists of ten sub-catchments (SC01-SC10), six storage units (Tank 1-6) with a total 
volume of 5,900 m3 and four CSO structures without storage capacity, for characteristics see Table 1 and Table 
2 (Schütze et al. 2017). The model represents a city with a total impermeable area of 180.20 ha with 35,000 
inhabitants each contributing with a dry weather flow representing the domestic flow and the inflow-
infiltration. The hypothetical city is located along two rivers, the Main River and a smaller, more sensitive 
Park Creek, serving the purposes of receiving watercourses (Schütze et al. 2017). A conceptual model of 
Astlingen is presented on Figure 7 below. 
 
Figure 7: A conceptual model of the Astlingen benchmark model. Provided by (Schütze et al. 2017). 
==PAGE:27==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 14 of 88 
 
 
10 years historically long-term rain series, from four rain gauges, is spatially distributed over the 10 sub-
catchments, provided by the Erftverband Water Association, see Table 1. The historically average annual 
rainfall at the four rain gauges (Erft1-4) is 705 mm, 723 mm, 699 mm and 711 mm, respectively (Schütze et 
al. 2017). The rain series has a time resolution of 5 minutes 
 
3.2 Astlingen SWMM model 
The model forming the basis of the model used in the case study conducted in this thesis,  is a yet unpublished 
implementation of the ‘Astlingen’ benchmark model in the open-source software SWMM provided by (Sun et 
al., n.d.). The original ‘Astlingen’ benchmark model was implemented in the Simba# simulator (Schütze et al. 
2017).  
Simplifications and uncertainties regarding the network details (e.g. the topology between the storage units), 
conducted in connection with the implementation of the model in SWMM, results in minor deviations in the 
results obtained compared to the original model in Simba#. In an evaluation conducted by the implementor of 
the SWMM model, the deviations was found be less than 10%, thus considered valid to be use for same 
purposes as the orginal Astlingen benchmark model (Sun et al., n.d.).  
Besides the ten sub-catchments (SC01-SC10) and six storage units (Tank 1-6), the ‘Astlingen’ sewer network 
in SWMM consists of 23 manholes/nodes (J1-19), four CSO structures (CSO 7-10) and 23 pipes (C1-23) with 
a total sewer length of 6.929.5 meters. The outflow of every storage unit is controlled by orifices (V1-V6) 
(with the number corresponding to the name of the related storage unit, e.g. the outflow of Tank 1 is controlled 
by orifice V1, Tank 2 by orifice V2, etc.). In the SWMM model, the sub-catchments are defined as 100% 
impervious, hence manning’s number and the depression depth represented in Table 1, is only for impervious 
area.  
The CSO structures and manholes are assigned as nodes with maximum depths of 1.2-2 m, and 0-2 meters, 
respectively. The discharge from the system to the WWTP is in SWMM defined as an outfall node with a fixed 
elevation of 0 m.   
The long-term rain series are assigned as time series to the rain gauges. No snow is included in the ‘Astlingen’ 
SWMM model, thus denoted rainfall rather than precipitation.  The SWMM catchment is presented in Figure 
8.

3.3 Models provided (literature) 
The provided SWMM model has been used to illustrate the two model approaches (Sun et al., n.d.): the 
benchmark model containing a suggestion for a good static control with local throttle controls (SC) and a 
model based on the principle of Equal Filling Degree (EFD) using simple rule-based control based on Equal 
Filling Degree (EFD) is a control strategy for which the aim is to fill the storage units equally by controlling 
the throttle outflow from the storage units based on a comparison of the water levels in the storage units in the 
system. For the Astlingen benchmark model, the EFD controls 4 of the 6 storage units in the system: Tank 2, 
Tank 3, Tank 4 and Tank 6. The system for which the EFD is developed uses the ‘nominal’ throttle flow if the 
water levels in all the storage units are below a defined threshold of 20 %. Exceeding the threshold, the storage 
units will be emptied by either limiting or increasing the throttle flow to the minimum or maximum throttle 
flow (Table 3)  (Sun et al., n.d.). In the provided SC model, the maximum outflow of the storage units is limited 
to the nominal flow presented in Table 3.

units.

3.3.1 The Base Case (BC) 
The Base Case defined as reference model for the RTC applied in the later sections. It is based upon this model 
all applied RTC strategies will be evaluated.  
The throttle flow from the storage units in the Base Case, will be the maximum throttle flows presented in 
Table 2. The choise of applying the mhe maximum throttle flow rather than the nominal flow, is ensuring the 
orifice opening (which limits the flow) is fully open in the Base Case, thus not limiting the RTC potential 
considered for the Base Case. A comparison between the Base Case with maximum throttle flows, and the SC 
model using the nominal flow, is presented for Tank 6 in Appendix F.   
As described earlier in section 2.3.4 (Equation 4), the maximum flow through an orifice depends on Bernoulli’s 
equation and a orifice discharge coefficient (C). In the application of the maximum throttle flow, the provided

4 SELECTING RAINFALL EVENTS FOR THE ESTIMATION OF 
CONTROL PARAMETERS 
Section 4 contains descriptions of methods and results related to the selection of rainfall events for further use 
in the estimation of Real Time Control (RTC) parameters. This includes selecting rainfall events considered 
relevant based on several defined criteria: definition and evaluation of simulation periods based on the relevant 
rainfall events and determination of one representative year from the ten years included in the long-term 
historical rain series. Lastly, the RTC potential and combined sewer overflow (CSO) locations in the Astlingen 
model will be identified for the representative year.  
All analysis and simulations conducted in this section are based on the Base Case defined for the fictive worked 
example ‘Astlingen’.  
4.1 Methodology  
4.1.1 Rain series 
The differences between simulating based on long-term rain series (or continuous rain) and single rainfall 
events are elucidated in this section. Long-term rain series are defined as multiple rain events occurring over 
a continuous period, in this case, years, whereas single rain events is an event occurring once within a shorter 
duration (Gironas, Roesner, and Davis 2009).  
Simulations with long-term rain series creates the opportunity of statistical analyses, from which return periods 
can be estimated. The main disadvantage of simulations conducted based on long-term rain series, is the 
increased computation time. This can be avoided by simulating based on CDS rains (Chicago design storms), 
but it requires a certain knowledge of the system investigated, and is highly user depended, due to the rainfall 
event investigated needs to be selected based on appropriate return periods (for the catchment) and event 
duration. (Rossman and Huber 2016).  
Simulating single events does not account for important parameters, such as variation of rain patterns (duration 
and intensities) or time between events. Time between will be of particularly high importance in the 
determination of good control management, due to impacts regarding infiltration (water soil content), available 
storage capacity (potentially limited by earlier events) and evaporation. These are all factors included when 
simulating based on long-term rain series. Based on this, simulations conducted using single events, requires 
more inputs of the initial conditions, and with higher accuracy(Gironas, Roesner, and Davis 2009). Based on 
this, long-term rain series are considered more accurate and robust in the evaluation of the hydraulic 
performance of an urban drainage system (Gironas, Roesner, and Davis 2009).  
==PAGE:31==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 18 of 88 
 
 
4.1.2 Selecting and simulating rainfall events of importance 
The aim of selecting relevant rainfall events from continuous historical long-term rain series is to reduce the 
computation time, thus increasing the efficiency of estimating real time control (RTC) parameters. For an event 
to be considered relevant, the rain within a given time must cause CSO from at least one storage unit present 
in the urban drainage system. In the selection of relevant events, controllable and non-controllable storage 
units are distinguished. The outflow rates from non-controllable storage units are assumed constant despite 
any control of the upstream part of the system, whereas the rate from controlled storage units depend on the 
applied control strategy. For a given storage unit, only the rain series of the sub-catchments directly 
contributing with flows to the storage unit are considered 
In the following section, the criteria for rainfall events to be considered relevant depending on the storage unit 
type (controllable or not) is detailed, along with a definition of the simulation periods for the relevant events. 
4.1.2.1 
Controllable storage units  
For controllable storage units, a rainfall event is considered relevant when the depth of rain falling on the 
upstream sub-catchment exceeds the maximum capacity of the storage unit. Thereby, the outflow rate is not 
taken into account in the selection of relevant rain events for the controllable storage units. A storage unit with 
a limited outflow could be a controlled storage unit with a (partially) closed orifice. Using this methodology 
therefore corresponds to a worst-case scenario. Hence, the maximum number of rainfall events is considered 
relevant, as smaller events otherwise not able to fill the storage unit are included. 
The depth of the storage unit (dSU) the rainfall depth must exceed is determined based on the volume of the 
storage unit (S) and the impervious area of the upstream sub-catchment (Aimperv) using the following Equation 
11.   
 
dSU [mm] =
S [m3]
Aimperv [m2] ∙1000 
Equation 11 
The total rainfall depth occurring within a certain duration is determined by the moving sum. Thereby, rain 
events for which the moving sum exceeds the storage unit depth (drain > dSU) are considered relevant. To avoid 
same event is considered more than once, e.g. by the moving sum exceeding the storage depth several times 
within same rainfall event, it is ensured that there is minimum of one timestep between the relevant events 
considered.  
The intensity of a rainfall event often increases proportionally with the event duration. With lower intensity in 
the beginning of a rainfall event, hence the moving sum might not exceed the rainfall depth at the beginning 
of an event. Since the further RTC-related simulations of the relevant events should include the entire event, 
the start of the relevant rainfall event is identified. 
4.1.2.2 
Non-controllable storage units 
For non-controllable storage units, the rainfall depth must exceed the depth of the throttle outflow from a 
storage unit within a given time, along with the maximum depth of the storage unit considered. The 
methodology is used for storage units considered not appropriate to control, e.g. due to a constant outflow rate 
from the storage unit independent of the upstream part of the system. By assuming all water upstream enters 
the storage unit simultaneously, the simulated scenario represents the worst-case scenario and ensures the 
maximum amount of rainfall events are considered relevant. In a real urban drainage system, all water from 
upstream will most likely not enter a storage unit simultaneously, especially not if any upstream parts of the 
system is controlled.  
Rainfall events with high intensities but short durations might affect the system more than events with low 
intensities and long durations. To account for this, several durations are considered for the outflow volume and 
in the selection of relevant rain events for non-controllable storage units.  
==PAGE:32==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 19 of 88 
 
 
The maximum depths (dSU) of non-controllable storage units are determined based on Equation 11. The throttle 
outflow (Qout) is multiplied with the durations of the rainfall events (D) considered to convert the throttle 
outflow to a volume of water leaving the storage unit. Dividing this with the upstream impervious area (Aimperv), 
a depth of the water leaving the system (dout) within a given time period (D) is determined. To include different 
durations, the total depth is determined for each duration considered using the following Equation 12.  
 
dout [mm] =
Qout [ m3
min] ∙D [min]
Aimperv [m2]
 
Equation 12 
The total depth (dtot) to be exceeded by the rain for an event to cause CSO, is the depth of the storage unit (dSU) 
and the depth of the water leaving the storage unit (dout) at different durations, see Equation 13.  
 
dtot = dout + dST 
Equation 13 
As for the function considering controllable storage units, the rainfall depth within a given time period is 
determined by the moving sum but considers several durations. Therefore, the rainfall depth within a given 
duration must exceed the total depth determined for the same duration (drain > dtot) to be considered relevant. 
Using several durations, a rainfall event is considered relevant when at least one criteria is met. The start of 
the events and the avoidance of overlaps are determined using the same method as described for controllable 
storage units.   
4.1.2.3 
Duration of relevant rainfall events  
The duration used in the section of the rainfall events reflect the time within which the rainfall depth must 
exceed the maximum depths of the storage units, hence the chosen durations are of high importance. For 
controllable storage units, the duration considered for the criteria of relevant rainfall events must be long 
enough for the events to cause CSO. Due to several factors storing water in the urban drainage system for 
longer periods is not desired. This includes WWTPs desiring to treat wastewater throughout the entire day to 
avoid exceeding the capacity of the WWTP during the day, while benefitting from the capacity of the WWTP 
being less loaded at night due to lower water consumption, along with avoiding methane formation in the 
stored water (Schmitt et al. 2019). 
Since the selection of relevant events is considered before simulating the system, one might have limited 
knowledge of the challenges in the system, hence the most critical time for the storage units are unknown. 
Within short time periods, only little water will enter and flow out of the storage units, hence short durations 
might not be considered relevant nor critical for the system. Whereas more water will enter the system 
considering longer time periods, but more water will also leave the system. For a storage unit with free 
capacity, it is not considered problematic that the inflow rate exceeds the outflow rate. The problem arises 
when the situation occurs for a filled storage unit leading to CSOs.  
4.1.2.4 
The simulation periods 
After determining the relevant rainfall events, the event periods are extended into simulations periods to 
consider factors potentially influencing the simulation of the urban drainage system.  
Even smaller rainfall events occurring within a certain time prior to the relevant events can impact the available 
capacity of the drainage systems during the relevant rainfall events. This can result in increasing risks of CSOs 
from the CSO structure and storage units, and flooding of the city. To make the model more realistic, these 
potential rainfall events are included in the simulations of the relevant events by extending the period 
simulation prior to the beginning of a relevant rainfall event. It is ensured that the chosen time prior to the 
event is not in the middle of a smaller rainfall event, but the entire period is included in the simulation period. 
If the chosen period prior to the relevant rainfall event is a dry period (without rainfall), this period is not 
included in the simulation period.  
==PAGE:33==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 20 of 88 
 
 
To ensure all stormwater within a relevant rainfall event is accounted for in the simulation of the urban drainage 
system, an extended time period is applied to the end of the relevant rainfall event simulated. The extended 
time length of the period applied depends on the time of concentration. The time of concentration depends on 
the overland flow time and the sewer flow time. The overland flow represents the time it takes from the rain 
falls on the sub-catchment until the stormwater enters the urban drainage system. This duration depends on the 
characteristics of the sub-catchment (surface roughness, slope and flow path length) and of the rainfall event 
(larger return periods causes faster overland flows). The sewer flow time is determined from the flow velocity 
and the length from the stormwater entering the sewers to the point of interest (e.g. a storage unit). The time 
of concentration can be calculated, or a conservative time period can be considered.  
 
4.2 Results and discussion 
4.2.1 Rain events of importance 
Tank 1 is considered relatively small comparing the volume of the storage unit with the impervious area of the 
upstream sub-catchment, which might result in frequent CSOs from the storage unit. This challenge the 
methodology used for selection of rain events for controllable storage units and the RTC potential of Tank 1. 
Downstream of Tank 1 is the WWTP located, which has a high capacity to receive water, hence the CSOs 
from Tank 1 might be bypass at the WWTP which happens notoriously often. Furthermore, due to the WWTP, 
the outflow rate from Tank 1 is assumed constant, independent of any control of the upstream storage units 
(Tank 2-6). Therefore, Tank 1 is considered a non-controllable storage unit in the selection of events and will 
not be controlled by RTC in the simulation of the system.  Tank 2-6 is considered controllable storage units, 
whereas Tank 1 is assumed non-controllable. 
4.2.1.1 
Exceedance depths  
For the non-controllable storage unit (Tank 1), rainfall event and outflow durations of 1, 6, 12 and 24 hours 
are considered. The depth of the throttle outflow assuming a constant outflow corresponding to the maximum 
throttle outflow (0.271 m3/s) from Tank 1 (see Equation 12), the maximum storage depth of Tank 1 (see 
Equation 11) and the total exceedance depths used as criteria is presented in Table 4.   
The rainfall depths are determined using rolling sum for the presented event durations. For Tank 1 and Tank 
3 the upstream sub-catchments are affected by rain series from more than one rain gauge, hence the rain series 
with the maximum annual water depth is used (rain gauge Erft 2 for both storage units).

4.2.1.2 
Simulation periods for the individual storage units 
In the following sections, terms of ‘rainfall event’ and ‘simulation period’ will be used. The definition of a 
rainfall event is from the rainfall event starts until it ends. Simulation periods are defined as a rainfall event 
periods extended with a time period before and after the event period.  
For the simulation periods, 8 hours are considered prior to the rainfall event start to ensure smaller events 
potentially impacting the available capacity of the urban drainage system is considered. If 8 hours prior to the 
rainfall event is a dry period without rainfall, the start of the simulation period is the start of the rainfall event. 
Only periods containing rainfall is applied prior to the event start. The time it takes to empty the storage units 
are unknown, thus a conservative choice of 24 hours after the end of the event periods are considered for the 
simulation periods. By extending the event period with a 24 hours dry period without any rain (0 mm) to the 
end of the event period, the simulations periods become very long (up to 20,5 days). To avoid too long 
simulations periods, the extended period applied after the event period is defined as a period where the total 
rainfall depths does not exceed 1 mm for a total duration of 24 hours. A ‘dry’ end criterion of 1 mm is chosen 
with the aim of including only rainfall events potentially causing CSO from the system, while ensuring the 24-
hour dry criteria is below the smallest 24-hour exceedance criteria determined for the storage units (1.52 mm 
in 24 hours for Tank 6).  
An identification of a simulation period for Tank 4 is represented on Figure 9. At 2000-01-24 20:55, the total 
precipitation within the past 24 hours has exceeded the criteria for which the rainfall event is considered 
relevant (4.29 mm). The rainfall for the event starts at 01-24 18:35, hence the event period begins at 01-24 
10:35 (8 hours prior to rainfall event start). The end of the rainfall event is identified based on 10 minutes 
without precipitation, identified at 01-25 02:25. The total precipitation depth from the event ends and 24 hours 
after is 1.89 mm, hence above the dry period criteria of 1 mm in 24 hours. The 24-hour dry period criteria are 
met 01-26 04:35, 26 hours after the end of the event. The identified event does not overlap with any other 
events. Thereby, the simulation period is identified from 24-01-2000 20:55 to 26-01-2000 04:35.

Simulation periods identified for the entire system

the relevant rainfall events, along with the inclusion of several rain series, results in overlaps between 
simulation periods identified for the respective storage units.  Simulation periods overlapping are extended to 
one period. The number of simulation periods identified for the respective storage units (Tank 1-6) per year 
and in total are presented in Table 6. Based on the exceedance depths and durations, 30-80 simulation periods 
per year are identified for the respective storage units. The number of simulation periods identified from the 
respective storage units are found strongly correlated with the total exceedance depth. Most simulation periods 
are found for storage units with lowest exceedance depths thus most periods identified for Tank 1 and Tank 6.

4.2.2 Evaluation of simulation periods  
In the following sections, the simulation periods will be analysed based on the rainfall events, combined sewer 
overflow (CSO) occurrences and rainfall events causing CSOs within the simulation periods. 
647 simulation periods result in a computation time exceeding 10 minutes without any control of the system. 
With the aim of reducing the computation time, it is investigated whether one year can be considered 
representative for all available 10 years. The simulations periods are determined to use for optimisation of 
RTC parameters, hence choosing a representative year should reflect the overall distribution of overflows 
events, including the magnitude of the overflow volumes and the rainfall events causing overflows. Therefore, 
an analysis of the rain events causing overflow and the overflow events is performed. A t-test is performed to 
test the representativeness of the individual years. An entire year is chosen to reduce the uncertainties related 
to seasonal variations, which might not be accounted for using half a year of a month as representative for the 
647 defined simulation periods. 
==PAGE:38==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 25 of 88

The yearly distributions of the total rainfall depth within events, event durations and intensities are presented 
in Figure 11.  
 
For the rainfall events identified within the defined simulation periods (without overlaps), 75% of the smallest 
rainfall event depths are in the range 0.02-0.34 mm considering all years. Considering the yearly distribution, 
75 % of the rainfall events within the simulation periods are smaller than 0.26-0.46 mm, indicating a variation 
between the years. 75% of the rainfall events within the defined simulation periods has a duration of 5-20 
minutes. This results in relatively low rainfall intensities for the majority of the events (75% in the range 0.16-
0.84 mm/hr). Despite of this, extreme outliers is found for all in investigated factors. Based on Pearson’s 
correlation coefficient, the event rainfall depths and event durations are strongly correlated (r = 0.83, indicating 
higher total rainfall depths occur for rainfall events with longer durations. Despite of the variations in rainfall 
depths, duration and intensities considering the yearly distributions, a high consistent is found between the 
values determined for the simulation periods considering all years and considering the individual years. 
Furthermore, the majority of the events occurring in the defined simulation periods are not considered 
extremes, but smaller rainfall events considering the events not included due to the definition of the simulation 
periods (less than the exceedance depths), thus considered suitable for RTC control. 
 
The third quantile (Q3) and the maximum 5 minutes intensities (1.56 mm/hr and 120.96 mm/hr) corresponds 
to a total rainfall depth of 0.13 mm and 10.08 mm occurring in 5 minutes, respectively. The 5 minutes intensity 
determined for the third quantile has a low return period (<0.36 years), thus expected to occur, whereas the 
maximum value is considered more extreme, corresponding to a return period of 20 years (Gregersen et al. 
2016). Same patterns are seen for the intensities occurring during 10, 15 and 30 minutes. The maximum 30-
min intensity determined considering all years (63.58 mm/hr) corresponds to 31.79 mm in 30 minutes, which 
exceeds the definition of a cloudburst (15 mm in 30 minutes). The maximum intensities found are much higher 
than the values determined for the third quantiles, hence only assumed to occur extremely rare. The Astlingen 
model is assumed representable for Central European conditions, hence in accordance with the rain expected 
for the geographically area. Nearly same tendencies and values are found for the intensities across the years.

1-2 % of the CSO volumes occurring from the system is not included in the defined simulation periods. CSO 
does not occur during 39% of the simulation periods, thus the exceedance depth criteria are not considered the 
reason. Furthermore, in the definition of the simulation periods the time considered prior and after the relevant 
rainfall events are chosen to ensure a minimum of 32 hours between rainfall events exceeding the depth criteria 
(8 hours prior and 24 hours ‘dry’ after the rainfall event). The wastewater is not expected to be stored in the 
urban drainage system for this long. Extreme rainfall events might overload the entire system for more than 
24 hours, thus the ‘dry’ period applied after the rainfall events is not enough, thus simulating an entire year 
will result in less available capacity in the storage units during the following events. Such an impact on the 
system is not included in the simulation of the simulation periods due to the initialization of the system.  
Nevertheless, the missing CSO volumes in the simulation periods are not expected to impact the RTC 
algorithms defined for the system due to the low difference (< 2 %).
