4.2.2.3 
CSO occurrences and CSO events 
In the following section, terms of ‘CSO occurrences’ and ‘CSO events’ will be used. CSO occurrences are 
defined as every CSO from a CSO structure, storage tank or manhole followed by at least two timesteps without 
CSO. This means that two CSO occurrences detected from same location will be defined as two independent 
occurrences if separated by 10 minutes without CSO. CSO events from the same location is defined as two or 
more independent events if separated by at least 6 hours without CSO. 6 hours is the time it approximately 
takes to empty the entire system, with no control added, considering a maximum throttle flow from Tank 1 to 
the WWTP of 271.28 L/s and a total storage volume (Tank 1-6) of 5,900 m3. Comparing the effects of 
controlling the system to the Base Case without control, 6 hours is assumed an appropriate definition of an 
overflow event (definition applied by (Sun et al., n.d.)).  
The primary purpose of defining CSO occurrences are for the determination of rainfall events causing CSO, 
whereas CSO events will be used to determine the effects of an applied RTC action. Due to the primary purpose 
of CSO events, 6 hours are chosen to consider the consequences of first flush related to a rainfall event in the 
evaluation of an applied RTC strategy, the definition of a rainfall event, including the time of concentration 
and time to empty the storage units.  
The CSO occurrences and events from the respective storage units are defined based on the performed 
simulation of the Base Case (section 4.2.2.2). During the 392 simulation periods with CSOs, 2711 CSO 
occurrences and 1462 CSO events occurred from the 6 CSO structures (Tank 1-6 and CSO 7-9) and manholes 
present in the model. The number of overflow occurrences and events from the respective locations are 
presented in Table 11. The total CSO volumes from year 2000 to year 2009 during the 392 simulation periods 
is 3,385,811 m3.  
 
Figure 13: The percentage of rainfall overflowing as CSOs [%], distribution per year and combined. 
VCSO/VRAIN [%] 
==PAGE:44==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 31 of 88 
 
 
Table 11: The number of CSO occurrences (separated by 10 minutes (2 timesteps)) and CSO events (using 6 hours separator) from 
different locations, CSO structures with storage capacity (Tank 1-6) and without (CSO 7-10) and manholes (Ji) during the 392 
simulation periods. 
During the simulation periods, the CSOs occur mainly from the storage units (Tank 1-6) and CSO structures 
(CSO 7-10). Furthermore, 3 manholes (J1, J4 and J15) are flooded meaning the city is flooded due to overload 
of the urban drainage system.  
The manholes and CSO structures are not designed with the aim of storing water, hence the maximum depths 
are 1-2 meters, which must be filled prior for flooding to occur. The CSO structures are located downstream 
of the sub-catchments with the purpose of effectively discharging water overloading the sewer system. These 
are expected to discharge water during heavy rainfall. The receiving area upstream of the storage units (Tank 
1-6) are significantly larger than the upstream area of the CSO structures. Therefore, CSO occurs nearly as 
frequent or more frequently from Tank 1-6 than CSOs from the CSO structures, despite of the maximum depths 
of the storage units are 5 meters, thus 3 meters more than the CSO structures.  
Flooding of the manholes occurs simultaneously with CSOs from the related downstream storage units (Tank 
1-6). This implies that the manholes are flooded due to surcharge caused by overload of the entire system, 
including the storage units located downstream. The flooding occurs within 5 minutes which is highly below 
the median CSO durations of 40 minutes. In accordance with the short durations, the flood volumes are 
significantly smaller than the CSOs from the CSO structures and storage units. The flood volumes from 
manhole J4 are in the range 0.4-1.4 m3, 71 m3 from manhole J1 and significantly larger from manhole J15 with 
a flood volume of 1,185 m3. The flooding of manhole J1 and J15 occurs simultaneously. Manhole J4 is located 
downstream of CSO structure CSO 7, manhole J1 is located upstream of Tank 5 and manhole J15 upstream of 
Tank 1. Tank 1 is relatively small compared with the area from which it receives water, thus small rainfall 
depths are expected to cause CSOs. Therefore, large CSO volumes are expected from Tank 1 and the upstream 
manhole J15. The total CSO volumes and durations of the CSO occurrences and events are presented on Figure 
14 and  Figure 15.

% is determined. On average, 5 CSO events occurs within each simulation period from 5 different locations. 
Considering the CSO occurrences, an average of 7 CSOs per simulation period, with each location overflowing 
twice during the simulation periods, expect for Tank 3. Tank 3 overflow twice as much, with an average of 4 
CSO occurrences from Tank 3 per simulation period. The reduction in number of CSOs considered along with 
the difference in the CSO volumes (Figure 14) highly indicates that many of the CSOs occurs within a short 
time period (less than 6 hours), thus caused by same rainfall event. This is particular visible for Tank 3 where 
the average CSO volume considering occurrences is nearly 37 m3, but approximately 1,670 m3 considering the 
CSO events. The CSO occurrences for Tank 3 occur within a relative short time period (average CSO duration 
of 10 minutes, see Figure 15 (only CSO occurrences)).

4.2.2.4 
Rainfall events causing CSOs 
A single rainfall event does not necessarily cause CSO, whereas consecutive rainfall events might result in 
CSOs. Based on the rainfall events identified within the simulation periods (section 4.2.2.1), an appropriate 
time period prior to the CSO occurrences are determined in the investigation of events potentially causing or 
contributing to CSOs of the urban drainage system.  
The appropriate time period to consider prior to a CSO occurrence depends on several factors, including 
characteristic of the rainfall event occurring (volume, duration and intensity), CSO volume caused, the time of 
concentration from the sub-catchment to the CSO location, the size of the upstream area, the storage volume 
to be filled with water prior for CSO to occur and the state of the remaining system during the CSO occurrence.   
For rainfall events with large intensities and short durations, less time must be considered prior to the overflow 
occurrences, whereas longer time periods must be considered for catchments with longer time of 
concentrations. Furthermore, a storage unit with a larger upstream area will most likely be filled faster than if 
the storage unit received water from a smaller area, assuming the same rainfall event (same depth), thus a 
shorter time period must be considered prior to the CSO occurrence. For storage units located more 
downstream in an urban drainage system, thus receiving water from one or more upstream storage units, the 
size of the storage unit and the upstream storage units will influence the time to consider prior to the CSO 
occurrence.  
The time to consider prior to the CSO occurrences are determined based on random sampling (of 
approximately 6 samples per storage unit), by investigating the durations between the CSO occurrences and 
till nearest rainfall event possibly causing the CSOs. Only rainfall events from the long-term rain series related 
to the sub-catchments of which the storage units receive water is considered. Tank 1 and Tank 3 are influences 
by more than one rainfall series, thus the series with most rainfall is considered. The rainfall patterns, 
magnitudes and occurrences of the 4 long-term rain series (Erft 1-4) are much alike due to the sub-catchments 
in the model being located geographically close, thus only insignificant differences are assumed considering 
only one rain series. The manholes flooded are not considered due to the low frequency of CSO occurrences 
(1-3), along with the assumption of the flooding occurring due to overload of the system as a result of the 
limited throttle outflow from the storage units located downstream of every manhole flooded.  
Results shows the time to consider prior to the overflow occurrences is between 0 min, meaning CSOs occurs 
during a rainfall event, and up to 45 minutes. The highest time differences between the CSO occurrences and 
the nearest rainfall event are found for Tank 3 and Tank 6, see Figure 16. All storage units are located relatively 
close the sub-catchment from which it receives water, thus the time to consider prior to the CSOs are not 
affected by large time of concentrations. By comparing the rainfall events occurring prior to the CSO 
occurrences with the CSO volume, CSOs occurring for the largest time difference (above approximately 30 
minutes) are assumed to be caused by overload of the system causing minor CSO volumes (after a larger CSO 
occurrence) rather than a rainfall event. Tank 1 is located furthest downstream in the urban drainage system, 
hence high time differences might be expected, but is not found due to the large upstream area from which it 
receives water.  
 
 
==PAGE:47==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 34 of 88 
 
 
 
Based on these assumptions, only rainfall events occurring within a maximum of 30 minutes prior to the CSO 
occurrences are considered rainfall events potentially contributing to the CSOs. Dry weather flows occur in 
the system, thus not only stormwater fills the storage units, although constituting a smaller proportion. For 
rainfall events where CSOs occur less than the considered time into the defined simulation period (< 30 
minutes), all rainfall occurring from the start of simulation period to end of the CSO occurrence are considered 
to contribute to the CSO. For 174 of the identified CSO occurrences (out of a total of 2711 CSO occurrences), 
the CSOs are not found depended on rainfall events occurring within the investigated time prior to the CSOs 
but are assumed to be from overload of the system, e.g. downstream of the storage unit or minor CSO volumes 
as a result of larger rainfall events.  The majority of the overflow events found independent is from the 
frequently overloaded Tank 3 (86 % of the 174 independent events, 12 % is for Tank 1 and the remaining 2% 
from Tank 5 (all minor CSO occurrences)).  
By assuming a constant of 30 minutes prior to the CSO occurrences, independent of the intensities, magnitudes 
or durations of the rainfall events occurring, poses a risk of not including all rainfall filling the storage units 
but only considers the final rainfall events leading to the CSO. The evaluation of rainfall events causing CSOs 
are based on a yearly distribution rather than considering the rainfall events relative to the individual storage 
units and CSOs. Therefore, the assumption of 30 minutes applies to all CSO occurrences and is considered 
general, thus decreasing the bias towards any storage unit occurring in the model (e.g. the storage units with 
the smallest time of concentration). Furthermore, considering additional 15 minutes prior to the CSOs 
occurrences does not result in significantly benefits as a maximum of 3 more CSOs events could be included. 
Figure 16: Rainfall [m3] occurring prior to an CSO occurrence [m3] for Tank 5 (top) and Tank 6 (bottom). The rainfall events causing 
CSOs occurs simultaneously with and 15 minutes prior for Tank 5 and Tank 6, respectively. The rainfall highlighted with red is the 
rainfall event included considering any rainfall event occurring 30 minutes prior to the CSO.  
==PAGE:48==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 35 of 88 
 
 
Depth of rainfall events causing CSOs [mm] 
To avoid the disadvantages of including too many rainfall events potentially not contributing to CSOs nor 
filling of the storage units, 45 minutes prior to the CSO occurrences are not considered.  
In the determination of rainfall events causing CSOs, the CSO volumes considered are based on a system 
without RTC, thus throttle flows are the only limitation in the system. Therefore, water enters and leaves the 
system during and prior to the CSO occurrences. Therefore, considering too long periods prior to the CSO 
occurrences causes a risk of including too many events not contributing to the CSOs. It is unknown the exact 
proportion of each rainfall event contributing to the CSOs. Therefore, the cumulative sum of the rainfall within 
the events are chosen to reduce the risks of small single events incorrectly being assumed to cause CSOs. The 
disadvantage of this method is the risk of including too many rainfall events. For rainfall events with high 
intensities and short durations, along with storage units where less time difference is found during the random 
sampling, the 30-minutes assumption might include too many events not causing the overflow, but general 
assumptions are chosen to simplify the process. Furthermore, the conducted analysis of the rainfall events 
within the defined simulation periods shows that the rainfall events are mainly smaller events, hence 
considering a few rainfall events not causing or contributing significantly to the CSOs is assumed to be of 
minor importance.  
The determination of the exact rainfall events causing CSOs could be conducted based on the time it takes to 
empty the storage units, while considering the dry flow. Due to the primary purpose of evaluating the rainfall 
events causing CSOs to conduct a comprehensive selection of one representative year for the available long-
term rain series, for further use in the estimation of RTC parameters, the estimations and general assumptions 
performed are assumed acceptable.  
A boxplot of the cumulative depths of rainfall events occurring 30 minutes prior to the CSO occurrences, thus 
assumed to cause the CSOs, are presented in Figure 17.

causing overflow. Extreme outliers are excluded for visual reason.

The minimum rainfall depths causing CSOs are found relatively equal throughout the years (0.03-0.04 mm). 
The first quantiles are investigated in the determination of a representative year due to RTC being most 
beneficial regarding smaller rainfall events and not extreme events.  
The first quantiles of the cumulative sum of the rainfall depths occurring for rainfall events maximum 30 
minutes prior to the CSO occurrences ranges from 2.3 mm to 3.3 mm distributed throughout the years, with a 
median depth of 2.8 mm. For the years 2000 and 2004, the first quantiles of the rainfall depths causing CSOs 
are equal to the median. The maximum CSO volumes considering the Q1 rainfall depths varies widely from 
786 m3 in 2002 and up to 5,756 m3 in 2008, with a median of 1,910 m3. The median is found closest to the 
maximum CSO volumes determined for the years 2003, 2006 and 2009. The median of the minimum and 
maximum durations of the Q1 rainfall events are 5 minutes and 4.5 hours, respectively. The median minimum 
and maximum intensities are 0.3 mm/hr and 16.0 mm/hr, respectively. These determined medians are close to 
the intervals determined for the years 2003, 2004, 2006 and 2007.   
4.2.2.5 
T-test 
Using a two-sample independent (Welch) t-test, it is investigated whether simulation periods determined for 
one year can be considered representative for all defined simulation periods distributed over 10 years. The 
investigation is based on the CSO volumes determined from the simulation conducted of the Base Case model. 
A null hypothesis (H0) and an alternative hypothesis (HA) is formulated. The null hypothesis is the mean values 
of the two independent groups are statistically equal, hence follows the same distribution, whereas the 
alternative states a statistical difference in the mean values, see Equation 14.  
 
H0: μ1 = μ2  
HA: μ1 ≠ μ2   
Equation 14 
Based on a 5% significant level (α), the null hypothesis is either rejected or accepted. The two groups tested 
are considered approximately normal distributed and independent due to the CSO events considered in the 
respective groups are different from each other. With group 2 being the CSO volumes determined for the 
simulation periods identified for the investigated year, group 1 represents the CSO volumes determined for the 
simulation periods identified for the remaining nine years. The sample sizes are of different sizes, has unequal 
variances and contains a large number of observations. The t-value for unequal variances are determined using 
a built-in function in python (see script).   
The results of the performed two-sample independent t-test for different sample sizes and unequal variances, 
using a significant level of 5% are presented in Table 13.

events from long-term historical rain series

each year (sample size, Ni) are denoted 1 for all years execpt the one investegated and 2 for the investagted year. The p-value and

With 95% confidence it can be concluded that, the difference between the mean CSO volume found when 
simulating with one of the given years or simulated using all years, will lie within the determined confidence 
interval. For all years, the p-values determined for the individual years are greater than the investigated 5% 
level of significance. T-values were determined (see appendix for the performed t-test), from which same 
conclusions can be drawn. All the determined p-values are above 0.1, indicating that there is none or little 
evidence against the null hypothesis. Based on this, it can be concluded that here is no sufficient evidence to 
conclude that there is a significant difference between the mean value of the overflow volumes occurred during 
the investigated years and all other available CSO years, and the individual years are found representative for 
all other years. 
Suggesting with 84% confidence that there are no significant differences between the year 2004 and all the 
remaining years of data combined. This means the null hypothesis can be rejected and suggest the overflow 
volumes in 2004 were not significantly different from the remaining 9 years worth of data. However, the 
confidence intervals are large giving less confidence in the results.  
 
4.2.3 One representative year 
Based on the analyses conducted, the simulation periods identified for the year 2003 are considered in high 
accordance with the results found from the remaining nine years considering a yearly distribution and 
combined (2000-2009):  
- 
The minimum, maximum and the first-, second- and third quantile determined for the total depths, 
durations and intensities of the rainfall events occurring within the identified simulation periods for 
year 2003 are in high accordance with the remaining years without significant extremes. 
  
- 
63 simulation periods are identified for year 2003. CSOs occurs during 62% of the simulation periods, 
covering 99% of the CSO volumes determined for the entire year 2003. This is in high accordance 
with the values determined considering the individual years and combined. 
==PAGE:51==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 38 of 88 
 
 
- 
Considering the individual simulation periods occurring for year 2003, the distribution of the total 
CSO volumes and rainfall depths appears narrower than found for some of the other years. This is 
considered a benefit regarding the estimation of the RTC parameters where too extremes are not 
considered appropriate in the optimisation.  
 
- 
For the identification of rainfall events causing CSOs, the same minimum rainfall depths are 
determined for nearly all years, whereas the first quantile depths of the year 2003 are close to the 
median value determined. For the durations and maximum CSO volumes related to the first quantile 
rainfall depth, the values are slightly below average, whereas the intensities of the rainfall events 
covers the entire range from the minimum value determined for the years and up to the slightly above 
the remaining years.  
 
- 
Finally, the p-value determined for year 2003 in the conducted t-test is 0.21, hence indicating year 
2003 with a 5 % level of significance can be considered representative for the remaining years, thus 
the p-value is in the lower end of the determined p-values. Furthermore, despite of the large 95%-
confidence intervals found for all years, the confidence interval for year 2003 does not stand out 
negatively compared to the other years, as the interval is considerable narrower than found for other 
years.  
Based on this, there is confidence in using the year 2003 as a representation of the dataset and applying this to 
future models for this analysis.  
 
4.2.4 Investigation of the RTC potential 
The following analyses will be based on the representative year 2003. The locations at which CSO occurs are 
identified. For the representative year, CSO occurs during 39 of the 63 identified simulation periods (62%), 
see section 4.2.2.2. The total CSO volumes during the simulation periods are in the range 12 m3 to 43,031 m3. 
There are identified 167 CSO events in the Base Case (BC) system (static control) during the year 2003, with 
a median CSO volume of 444.6 m3. Considering the individual simulation periods, CSO occurs from 1 to 11 
different locations, with a median of 4 locations per simulation period.

Weak or none correlations are determined between the length of the individual simulation periods and the other 
investigated parameters (CSO volume, CSO events nor the number of CSO locations), hence it is assumed

correlation of 0.64 is found between the CSO volume and the total number of CSO events occurring within 
the simulation periods. A strong correlation is found between the number of CSO events during the simulation 
periods and the amount of locations from which the CSO occurs (0.92). This emphasizes the finding of CSOs 
rarely occurring from the same location more than once during a simulation period.  
The knowledge gained from investigating the correlations will be used in the selection of simulation periods 
considered for the estimation of RTC parameters. Knowing the length of the simulation periods are not 
affecting the CSO volumes nor the number of locations from which CSO occurs, there is no need to consider 
this in the selection. Whereas the moderate correlation between the CSO volumes and CSO events and CSO 
locations, respectively, indicates that considering simulation periods with a high number of CSO events from 
different CSO location to some extend ensure considering simulation periods causing larger CSO volumes.

4.2.5.1 
Correlations 
The rainfall events causing the individual CSO occurrences has been identified earlier, based on the rainfall 
events occurring within 30 minutes prior to the CSO occurrences. For the year 2003, the characteristics of the 
rainfall events causing CSOs are determined and connected to the individual CSO events and simulation 
periods. Thereby, the total precipitation depths, durations, intensities causing CSOs are identified for the 
respective simulation periods. These characteristics are analysed in order to select only a few representative 
simulation periods to use in the estimation of RTC parameters, by considering potential shared characteristics 
of the rainfall events causing overflow. 
For some simulation periods, there are several CSO occurrences from up to 11 different locations. In order to 
simplify the analysis and selection of representative simulation periods, only the maximum depths of the 
rainfall events causing CSO occurrences within each simulation period are used in the further analysis. By 
considering the CSO occurrence caused by the maximum rainfall depths in the estimation of RTC parameters, 
the control determined are assumed to impact the CSO occurrence caused by smaller events. Statistics of the 
maximum rainfall depths, and the related CSO volumes, event durations and intensities (for the entire event 
and within 5, 10, 15 and 30 minutes) for these rainfall events are presented in Table 15.

Figure 22: Pearson correlation coefficient determined the maximum rainfall depth causing CSOs and the total CSO volume for each 
simulation period, along with parameters related to the event with the maximum rainfall depth, such as the duration and (maximum) 
intensities  of the event, along with the CSO volume caused by the maximum depth). Following applies for the correlation coefficients

correlation.   
==PAGE:57==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 44 of 88 
 
 
A strong correlation is found between the maximum rainfall depth within a simulation period and the total 
CSO volume within the simulation periods (0.84) and the CSO volume caused by the maximum rainfall depth 
(0.88). Thereby, simulation periods with a higher maximum rainfall depth are likewise found to be periods 
with high CSO volumes, considering the entire simulation period and considering only the CSO occurrence 
related to the maximum rainfall depth.  
A moderate correlation of 0.67 is found between the maximum rainfall depth and the number of CSO locations. 
This indicates that more rainfall will only to some extend and, in some cases, result in overload of more than 
one location in the system. Furthermore, a strong correlation is found between the number of CSO locations 
and total CSO volume (0.79) indicating that higher CSOs volumes is a result of CSOs from more locations. 
The duration of the maximum rainfall depth is moderately correlated to the total CSO volumes of a simulation 
period (0.52), but strongly correlated to the CSO volume causes by the rainfall event (0.79) and the rainfall 
depth (0.79). This implies that higher maximum rainfall depths are to find for longer event durations and causes 
higher CSO volumes. Despite of this, no correlations are found between the maximum rainfall depths within 
the simulation periods and the intensities determined. The correlation between the intensities and durations are 
stronger, but weak. However, the correlation is weaker using the maximum intensities rather than the average 
intensities, which might be related to the average intensity decreasing with an increase in the event duration.  
Furthermore, there is none or very weak correlation between the maximum or average intensities within 5-, 
10-, 15- and 30-minutes and any of the remaining parameters. There are significant uncertainties related to the 
30-minutes intensities due to many of the rainfall events causing CSOs has duration less than 30 minutes, 
hence only a few data points are considered. The intensities determined for certain minutes are extreme 
correlated with each other. This is expected as the maximum or average 5-minutes intensity might be included 
in the 10-minutes intensity determined for the same event.   
 
Any common characteristics are considered in the selection of four events for further use in the optimisation 
of the RTC algorithms.  
 
5 ESTIMATING CONTROL PARAMETERS 
Section 5 contains descriptions of methods and results related to estimating real time control parameters. This 
includes a description of the software package, the optimisation tool and objective functions applied for the 
estimation. The analyses and results in this section, are conducted with the aim of investigating the potential 
impacts selection of simulation periods, control strategies and objective functions have on the estimated control 
parameters and the computationally efficiency. The results achieved are compared to literature findings 
regarding the worked example ‘Astlingen’. Lastly, the representativeness of the selected year, and the 
associated simulation periods, are investigated.  
5.1 PySWMM 
The Python language software package PySWMM allows interaction and modifications of USEPA SWMM5 
models. Thereby, control actions can be developed, automated and optimised by making use of packages 
available in Python. This gives the opportunity to change control actions based on hydraulic trends occurring 
during an execution of the model in SWMM, thus PySWMM is considered highly suitable for RTC of an urban 
drainage system. Aside from de- or increasing flows in sewer networks in the model through control actions, 
it is furthermore possible to change significant parameters in the SWMM model using PySWMM, including 
maximum node depth, simulation time steps or define exact periods to be simulated (McDonnell 2019). A 
detailed description of available PySWMM features are available in ‘PySWMM Documentation’ (McDonnell 
2019). 
==PAGE:58==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 45 of 88 
 
 
In addition to storing data (e.g. node depths, flows in links and water level in storage units) during a simulation, 
two files are generated after execution. For every simulation conducted, an out-file (.out) and a model result-
file (.rpt) are generated and saved in the folder containing the SWMM model. The files can be accessed by 
importing to PySWMM and text-reading programs (e.g. Notepad++). An out-file and result-file is generated 
for each of the individual simulation periods. The out-file contains timeseries data of the individual elements 
implemented in the SWMM model (every node, sub-catchment, storage units, pipes etc.). The model result 
file contains total values of the routing and runoff from the simulation periods, e.g. total CSO volume 
(flooding) of manholes, pipes and storage unit, or the total rainfall depth for the entire catchment.  
5.2 Optimisation 
To obtain beneficial RTC parameters, optimisation is included in the estimation of the parameters. The goal 
of optimising is to determine decision variables by min- or maximising a defined objective function. For this 
thesis, the optimisation is conducted using a python module ‘scipy.optimize’ and the Nelder-Mead simplex 
algorithm. The algorithm is considered one of the simplest methods, easily applicable and appropriate for 
simple minimisation problems due to its robustness. The algorithm does not use gradient evaluations, hence 
longer computation time might occur (The SciPy community 2019).The method is often applied to 
optimisation problems with unknown derivatives and discontinuities, i.e. non-linear problems. The Nelder-
Mead method is a ‘direct search method’ meaning the optimisation is based on comparison of the results of 
two functions, hence posing a risk of locating only local minimums (McKinnon 1998).  
The inputs to the optimisation function in Python include an objective function and an initial guess of the 
decision variable (x0). Additionally, the maximum number of iterations or function evaluations allowed can 
be specified in the optimisation function, among other options depending on the optimisation function used 
(McKinnon 1998). 
The result of the defined objective function is determined based on the input values. Therefore, the optimisation 
function continuously changes the decision variable (e.g. the control set point) until reaching a minimum output 
of the objective function or until exceeding the maximum number of iterations or function evaluations are 
reached. An objective function might contain more than one local minimum. Using other minimisation tools 
might minimise the risk of wrongly assuming a local minimum being the global minimum, e.g. by using basin 
hopping. Since this type of method results in excessive computation time, and cannot ensure a global minimum 
is reached, the negative consequences are found to exceed the positive and the model is therefore not used in 
this thesis.  
The interactions between Python and SWMM (denoted PySWMM) along with the actions performed during 
the optimisation are presented in Figure 23. 
 
Figure 23: Interactions and actions performed for the optimisation of an objective function. Based on the set point (an estimated RTC 
parameter), control actions are defined in Python (e.g. Orifice V4 must close when the water level in Tank 1 exceeds 1 meter).  
PySWMM interacts with SWMM, thus a simulation is conducted. When the sensor located in the urban drainage system monitored the 
==PAGE:59==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 46 of 88 
 
 
set point, the controller will regulate the actuator according to the control action. Based on the results of the simulation, the result of 
the objective function will be determined and evaluated. Depending on the evaluation, a new set point is determined by the optimiser, 
thus new control actions are defined. This continues until the minimum result of the objective function is reached.  
In this project where the aim is to estimate RTC parameters, the continuously changed decision variable 
represents the control set point (K). Whenever the depth of an investigated storage unit (SU) is equal to or 
above the control set point, the setting of the orifice (O) changes by closing (setting = 0). The orifice setting 
value defines the opening fraction of the controlled orifice. Thereby, a control setting of 0 defines a completely 
closed orifice and a setting of 1 indicates a completely open orifice.  
The control actions defined for the optimisation in Python: 
IF Tanki water level ≥ K  
THEN Oi setting = 0  
ELSE Oi setting = 1 
Controlling orifice Oi based on Tanki  
The optimisations conducted involves several independent simulation periods. Therefore, for every decision 
variable investigated, an out-file (.out) and a model result-file (.rpt) is generated for every independent 
simulation period. The generated files (.out and .rpt) are saved with the generic names containing the set point 
investigated and a different number for every simulation period investigated. (K{K}Evn{Event no}, e.g. with 
three simulation periods, the first will be saved as Evn0, second Evn1 and last Evn3).  
5.2.1 Objective function 
Based on the decision variable, the objective function is determined as a single scalar value to be optimised. 
The following RTC objectives have formed the basis of the developed objective functions: 
- 
Minimise the accumulated overflow volumes 
- 
Minimise the number of overflow events 
- 
Minimise CSOs to the Main River and Park Creek, along with flooding of the city 
The following three objective functions are investigated and optimised through the minimisation:  
1) minimising the CSO volumes to the Main River and Park Creek, along with flooding volume of the city,  
2) minimising the CSO volumes to the Main River and Park Creek, along with flooding volume of the city and 
minimising CSO events,  
3) minimising the accumulated CSO volumes. 
The considerations for the objective functions are described below, using the following letter definitions: V is 
the CSO and flooding volumes, f an applied factor and Nov the number of CSO events. 6 hours without CSO 
from the system is used as an event separator.  
Objective Function 1: Minimising the CSO volumes to the Main River and Park Creek, along with 
flooding of the city 
Minimize ∑(VCSOs,T1 ∙fT1 + VCSOs,MR ∙fMR + VCSO,PC ∙fPC+Vflood,C ∙fC) 
Flooding of the city (manholes) is considered most damaging regarding discharge locations due to the related 
costs, environmental and health risks. Therefore, flooding of any manholes is assigned the highest factor in the 
objective function to be optimised. Park Creek is smaller, thus more sensitive than Main River, hence the 
higher factor. Tank 6 is the only storage unit discharging to Park Creek. The remaining storage units discharge 
to Main River, hence reducing CSOs of Tank 6 is prioritised. Tank 1 is located near the WWTP, hence a 
differentiation is made between the CSOs from Tank 1 (T1) and Tank 2-5 (MR). The factors applied to the 
different discharge points are presented in Table 16. 
==PAGE:60==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 47 of 88 
 
 
Table 16: Optimising the applied objective function (Objective Function 1), the aim is to minimise the CSO volumes discharged to 
Main River (MR), the more sensisitve Park Creek (PC) and most crusial  flooding of the city. The damages related to the different 
locations is accounted for by applying the a factor, depending on the discharge point of the CSOs. 
Discharge point 
Location 
Factor (fi) 
Near WWTP (T1)

Objective Function 2: Minimising the CSO volumes to the Main River and Park Creek, along with 
flooding of the city, and minimising CSO events 
Minimize (∑(VCSOs,T1 ∙fT1 + VCSOs,MR ∙fMR + VCSO,PC ∙fPC+Vflood,C ∙fC) ∙NOV) 
Additional to the consideration of the CSO discharge point described for Objective Function 1, Objection 
Function 2 takes the number of CSO events into account. The number of CSO events are considered in the 
optimisation of the objective function, in order to account for the impacts of first flush and dilution.  
 
Objective Function 3: Minimising the accumulated CSO volumes. 
Minimize ∑(VCSOs,T1 + VCSOs,MR + VCSO,PC+Vflood,C) 
The objective function depends only on minimising the CSO volume, independent of location and events. This 
objective function is mainly constructed for comparison with Objective Function 1 and 2. Thereby, the 
objective function can be used as an indicator of the impacts of estimating and optimising RTC parameters 
while considering and applying factors.  
 
Based on inputs containing start and end dates of the periods to be investigated, along with a control set point 
(K) the simulations are conducted, restarting and initialising the system every time the end of a simulation 
period is reached. Due to the definition of the simulation periods (i.e. a dry period prior to any rain event 
occurring in the simulation period, see section 4.2.1.1), the initialisation is not considered to apply any further 
uncertainties to the model, e.g. by neglecting any rain occurring during the initialisation. Since dry weather 
flow is a part of the system, a single timestep simulated at the beginning of a simulation period will correspond 
to a dry period. The objective function extracts timeseries of the overflow [m3/s], using the defined simulation 
time resolution, from individual location (CSO structure, storage unit and node) in the model from the 
generated out-files related to the investigated control set point (K). The time series (e.g. containing the flooding 
volume, could also be appended during the simulation, but this increases the computation time.   
 
5.3 Results and discussion  
5.3.1 Selection of simulation periods for the optimisation 
To accomplish computationally efficient estimation of RTC parameters, a few simulation periods are selected 
from the 62 simulation periods identified for the representative year of 2003. Computationally efficiency 
includes reducing the computation time related to estimating sufficient RTC parameters and control strategies. 
Even though the simulation periods selected are chosen from the simulation periods identified for the 
representative year of 2003, the RTC parameters estimated based upon the selected periods, must be 
representative for all available years.    
==PAGE:61==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 48 of 88 
 
 
A strong correlation was found between the maximum rainfall depth within a simulation period and the total 
CSO volume (Figure 22). Therefore, the maximum depth within the simulation period forms the basis of the 
selection of simulation periods used for the further development of control strategies and estimation of RTC 
parameters.   
5.3.1.1 
Dividing simulation periods into groups 
The defined simulation periods are divided into six groups based on the maximum rainfall depth determined 
for the events occurring 30 minutes prior to the CSO occurrences, see Table 17: One group considers all 
simulation periods with smaller CSO volume, with a maximum precipitation depth equal to or less than the 
first quantile (Q1) of 3.7 mm. Another group (Q2) considers simulation periods with a maximum precipitation 
depth greater than the first quantile, but equal to or less than the second quantile (3.7 mm < Pmax ≤ 7.4 mm), 
and a third group (Q3) considers simulation periods with a maximum depth in the range of the second and 
third quantile (7.4 mm < Pmax ≤ 10.6 mm). The rational for this selection is to investigate the representativeness 
of the rainfall events impacting the control actions and how these control actions affect the CSO volumes, 
when considering other events than developed for. The remaining three groups considers all simulation periods 
less than or equal to the second quantile and third quantiles, respectively. Hence considering simulation periods 
with a maximum depth corresponding to 50% and 75% of the defined simulation periods. The last group 
considers all simulation periods with a maximum depth larger than the first quantile but smaller than the third. 
Simulation periods with maximum precipitations depth larger than the third quantile are not considered, since 
the 25% largest rainfall events are assumed to include extreme events, for which RTC is not considered 
beneficial nor able to reduce the CSO volumes or frequency. The six groups are illustrated graphically in 
Figure 24.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
5.3.1.2 
Control action 
The control action applied to evaluate the results of six defined groups will be described in this section.   
Storage unit Tank 4 is the controllable location causing least CSOs considering all years combined, 2003 and 
the simulation periods identified for 2003. Storage unit Tank 1 located downstream of Tank 4 is the most 
frequently overloaded part of the system, thus reducing the outflow from Tank 4 to Tank 1 might potentially 
reduce the CSO volumes from Tank 1. Therefore, Tank 4 is considered to have a high RTC potential. To 
reduce the outflow of Tank 4 to Tank 1, a control action is applied to the system. By locating a sensor in Tank 
1 monitoring the water level in the storage unit in real time, the outflow of Tank 4 can be limited by controlling 
orifice V4 based on the water level.  
The orifice setting represents the opening fraction of the orifice, thus an orifice setting of 0 corresponds to a 
completely closed orifice with not outflow from Tank 4, while a setting of 1 indicates the orifice is fully open 
with a maximum throttle flow of 80 L/s. 
The control action of the feedback control loop: 
IF Tank 1 water level >= K  
THEN Orifice V4 setting = 0  
Q2

5.3.1.3 
Objective function 
The optimal set point (K) the water level in Tank 1 must reach for orifice V4 to close, is optimised based on 
the objective function minimising the CSOs discharged to the Main River (MR and T1) and Park Creek (PC), 
and flooding of the city (C). Applying Objective Function 1, the optimisation accounts for the discharge point 
by considering the damages related to the CSO location, without considering the number of CSO events.  
Objective Function 1 with the applied factors: 
Minimize ∑(VCSOs,T1 ∙fT1 + VCSOs,MR ∙fMR + VCSO,PC ∙fPC +Vflood,C ∙fC) = 
Minimize ∑(VCSOs,T1 ∙1,000 + VCSOs,MR ∙2,000 + VCSO,PC ∙5,000 +Vflood,C ∙6,000)

investigated.

Considering the simulation periods with the smallest maximum rainfall depth (≤ 3.7 mm) result in an optimal 
set point of 3.4 m. This is significantly smaller than the optimal set point of 4.9 m, found when not including 
the simulation periods with the 25% smallest depths (> 3.7 mm). A set point of 4.9 m means that orifice V4 
will close when the storage unit Tank 1 is 98% filled with water (max depth of 5 m).  Whereas a set point of 
3.4 means the orifice will close whenever the storage unit Tank 1 is 68% filled with water. It is often considered 
the best solution to have a set point as high as possible, while reducing CSOs, to ensure least water necessarily 
is stored in the urban drainage system. Storing water in the system increases the risk of sediment deposition as 
a result of smaller flows.

The largest difference between the reduction of CSO volume achieved for the simulation periods, year 2003 
and all years, is found for the control set point optimised considering the simulation periods with the 25% 
lowest rainfall depths (Q1).  
For all RTC parameters applied and for all periods considered, controlling the outflow of Tank 4 result in an 
increase of the CSO volume from Tank 4. For the BC considering the simulation periods, no CSO occurs from 
Tank 4, thus any CSOs from Tank 4 result in an 100% increase of the CSO volume. For year 2003, the increase 
in CSO volume from Tank 4 is 23% less considering a set point of 4.9 m (group Q2, Q3 and Q2+Q3), compared 
to a set point of 3.4 m (27,720 m3) (Q1, Q1+Q2 and Q1+Q2+Q3).  
Despite the increased CSO volumes from Tank 4, applying control of the outflow from Tank 4 based on the 
water level in Tank 1, result in an overall reduction in CSO volumes from the entire system. This implies that 
that the control action applied is suitable and fulfils its objective of reducing the CSO volumes from the entire 
system. The largest reductions achieved are regarding the CSO volumes from Tank 1 (23-26 %). In contrast 
to the trend found for Tank 4, the reductions in CSO volumes from Tank 1 are largest using the set point 
determined including the first quantile (26%). The annual reduction of the CSO volume for 2003 is 136,969 
m3, which is 3 % less than applying a set point of 4.9 m.  
Comparing the reduction potential achieved considering the simulation periods and the reduction achieved for 
year 2003 and all years, indicates an overestimation of the potential. The reductions found considering the 
simulation periods for group Q1 (max P ≤ 3.7 mm) implies a significant overestimate of the reduction potential. 
A total reduction of 55 % considering the simulation periods are significantly larger than the reductions of 8% 
and 7% considering the entire year 2003 and all available years (2000-2009). This tendency of overestimation 
is found for any simulation period considering the first quantile. A smaller difference is found when 
considering additional simulation periods with a larger maximum precipitation depth (24% for simulation 
period Q1+Q2 compared to an annual and overall reduction of respectively 8% and 7%, or 15% considering 
Q1+Q2+Q3).  
For group Q2 (max P of 3.7-7.4 mm), a similar overestimation of the reduction potential is found. Considering 
the simulation periods, the reduction potential (20%) is nearly double the potential found simulating year 2003 
(10%) or all years (9%).  A similar overestimation is found considering group Q2+Q3, thus all simulation 
periods with a maximum rainfall depth range of 3.7-10.6 mm 
The simulation periods with a maximum rainfall depth in the range 7.4 mm - 10.6 mm (group Q3) are found 
most representative regarding the CSO reduction potentials. The reduction of CSO from Tank 1 is 24% 
considering the simulation periods, only 1% more than for 2003 and all available years. The overall differences 
in the reduction potentials are due to CSOs from Tank 4, manhole J15, which is only flooded when all years 
ae considered (accounts for 1%), and smaller insignificant differences (<0.1%) for other locations. For group 
Q3, the optimal set point of 4.9 m result in reductions of 10,976 m3 overflowing from Tank 1, but an increase 
of 3,125 m3 more from Tank 4 during year 2003.   
5.3.2 Selecting three simulation periods 
Based on this analysis, simulation periods with a maximum precipitation depth of 7.4 mm - 10.6 mm (group 
Q3) is used for further analysis. With the aim of increasing the computationally efficiency, including 
minimising the computation time related to estimating RTC parameters, three simulation periods from the most 
representative group (Q3) are selected for the further optimisation of the urban drainage system. The CSO 
volumes of the 10 simulation periods included in group Q3 are presented in Figure 26, for the BC and the 
applied control action (V4).  
==PAGE:65==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting

controlled based on the water level in Tank 1 (closes when the water level in Tank 1 reaches 4.9 m). The simulation periods are within 
the most representative group (Q3, the maximum rainfall depth of the events causing a CSO occurrence is 7.4 mm - 10.6 mm)  
The reduction of the CSO volumes achieved for the individual simulation periods in group Q3, ranges from 
10% to 30%, with the overall reduction (comparing the total CSO volumes) of 14%. The control leads to a 
decrease in CSO volumes from Tank 1, but an increase from Tank 4, hence significantly smaller CSO volumes 
shows a total reduction is achieved. For group Q3 a relatively strong correlation (r = 0.7) was found between 
the reduction achieved for the applied control action, and the CSO volumes and CSO locations. Therefore, the 
three simulation periods selected for the further analyses, are the three simulation periods causing the largest 
CSO volumes: 2003-8-10 (3rd), 2003-11-03 (8th), 2003-12-10 (10th). The same optimisation that was performed 
for all ten simulations within group Q3 (controlling orifice V4 based on the water level in Tank 1) is performed 
for the three selected simulation periods to ensure the periods can be considered representative for the entire 
group. The results are based on the reduction achieved and the optimal set points determined. The result of 
optimising using all ten events defined for Q3 and optimising based on the three selected simulation periods 
are presented in Figure 27.

Optimisation based on the entire group Q3 resulted in an optimal set point of 4.9 m, which is the water depth 
to be monitored in Tank 1 for orifice V4 to close. The optimal value determined using the three selected 
simulation periods is 4.92 m, which results in the exact same reductions and CSO volumes for the individual 
locations. Furthermore, the total reduction achieved using all periods defined for Q3 is 14% compared to the 
Base Case with static control (without RTC). For the three simulation periods, a total reduction of 12% is 
achieved. The reduction is smaller than using all events for Q3, but more similar to the reductions achieved 
simulating the entire year 2003 (9%) and all available years (10 %), using the exact same control action (orifice 
V4 based on Tank 1). Therefore, the three selected simulation periods are considered representative for the 
group and will be used for the further control and optimisation of the system.  
The duration of the three selected simulation periods are 3 days and 19 hours (2003-8-10), 3 days and 20 hours 
(2003-11-03) and 4 days and 3 hours (2003-12-10). The durations of the three selected simulation periods are 
among the longest, considering the simulation periods identified for year 2003. Despite the CSO volumes 
forming the basis of the selection of three simulation periods, the durations are considered of minor importance 
due to the weak correlation determined between the duration of the individual simulation periods and the CSO 
volume occurring.

event are caused by a rainfall event with a larger depth but within a much longer duration than the remaining 
two events. Despite the longer duration, the CSO occurs from one location more than for the reaming two 
simulation periods with larger intensities. Especially for the simulation period 2003-11-03, the intensities are 
significantly larger, but the total CSO volume is much smaller than caused by the periods with the longer 
duration.  
5.3.3 Control strategies 
In this section, two control strategies are applied in order to investigate the potential impact a strategy has on 
the optimal control parameter determined. The control strategies are developed to achieve the same operational 
objective of reducing CSOs from the urban drainage system. For Control Strategy 1, the control points are 
located where the maximum RTC potential are presumed based on the CSO volumes, see Table 14 and Figure 
20.  For Control Strategy 2, the control points are located with the aim of minimising the discharge to the 
sensitive Park Creek to the largest extent possible, hence reducing the CSOs from Tank 6, while trying to fulfil 
the overall objective of reducing the overall CSOs from the system.  
The optimal control parameters are investigated using the same objective function as used above, Objective 
Function 1 minimising the CSOs to the Main River and Park Creek, along with flooding of the city, by 
considering an applied factor: 
Objective Function 1 with the applied factors: 
Minimize ∑(VCSOs,T1 ∙fT1 + VCSOs,MR ∙fMR + VCSO,PC ∙fPC +Vflood,C ∙fC) = 
Minimize ∑(VCSOs,T1 ∙1,000 + VCSOs,MR ∙2,000 + VCSO,PC ∙5,000 +Vflood,C ∙6,000) 
The optimal set points are found by making use of an in-built optimisation module in Python and applying the 
Nelder-Mead simplex algorithm. 
5.3.3.1 
Control Strategy 1: utilise the RTC potential 
The strategy of Control strategy 1 is to utilise the maximum RTC potential by applying control points and 
limiting the outflow of storage units causing least CSO volumes, see Table 14 and Figure 20. The strategy is 
developed aiming to reduce the overload of T1 by limiting the outflow from Tank 2, 3, 4 and 5 to the 
downstream Tank 1, potentially increasing the free capacity in the pipes from Tank 6 to Tank 1, hence 
decreasing the discharge from Tank 6 to the sensitive Park Creek. Therefore, the outflow from Tank 6 is the 
last applied control point but is controlled to achieve the main objective of reducing the CSOs from the system 
to the maximum extent possible.  
The percentages each storage units account for considering the total CSO volume from the system during year 
2003 and all available years (2000-2009) are presented in Table 20. The total CSO volume for year 2003 is 
255,599 m3 and 2,280,049 m3 considering all years.

Throughout all available years, including 2003, Tank 4 is the storage unit contributing with the smallest CSO 
volume (5,604 m3). Therefore, the first control action is located with the aim of limiting the outflow of Tank 
4 by controlling orifice V4 (located at Tank 4) based on the water level in Tank 1. Tank 2 contributes with the 
second smallest volume (2 % of the total CSO volume, 1% for year 2003), hence the second control action is 
to control orifice V2 based on the water level in Tank 1, while controlling V4 based on the optimal set point 
found for Tank 1. Therefore, determining the optimal control set point using the objective function described 
above, is conducted while keeping the prior implemented control actions constant. The third control point 
applied is orifice V3, then orifice V5 and lastly orifice V6. Orifice V2-V5 are controlled based on the water 
level in the downstream Tank 1 and orifice V6 is controlled based on the water level in Tank 3, located 
downstream of V6.

For all considered control actions, the water levels to be reached for an orifice to close is near the maximum 
depths of 5 meters, indicating the entire system and especially the downstream Tank 1, is overloaded during 
the simulation periods. 
The maximum depth of the storage units Tank 1-6 is 5 meters, hence closing orifice V5 when the water level 
in storage unit Tank 1 reaches 5 meters corresponds to limiting the outflow of Tank 5 every time Tank 1 is 
completely filled and prior to a potential CSO occurrence. An optimal parameter this high (5 m) furthermore 
indicates Tank 5 is overloaded during the simulation periods, hence the optimal solution is to let as much water 
as possible flow out of the storage unit and not be stored.

Page 57 of 88 
 
 
None of the applied control actions impact the CSO volumes from the uncontrollable CSO structures (CSO 7-
10), which is expected due to the lack of storage capacity upstream of the CSO structures. Furthermore, all 
applied control actions result in reductions of the CSO volumes from Tank 1 (reductions > 10%).   
The applied control actions impact the system differently depending on the simulation period investigated. 
Applying the control action CL 1, orifice V4 is controlled based on the water level monitored in Tank 1, 
resulting in an increase of the CSO volume from Tank 4, but a reduction in the total CSO volume from the 
entire system, reductions of 10% - 14% for the simulation periods. Furthermore, for all results except one (CL 
4, additionally controlling V5), the applied control actions result in an increase of the CSO volumes from the 
storage unit which outflow is limited. For instance, for control action CL 1 the outflow of Tank 4 is limited 
resulting in an increase of the CSO volumes. For the BC, no CSO is detected from Tank 4, hence an increase 
of 100% only indicates CSO occurs after applying the control actions, but the CSO volumes are 9 m3, 18 m3 
and 1672 m3, respectively. Control action CL 4 of closing orifice V5 every time Tank 1 reaches 5 meters does 
not impact the system hence the control action could be omitted, and same reductions would be achieved.  
The large differences of how the individual control actions impact the three simulation periods highlights the 
importance of considering more than one simulation period during the optimisation of RTC parameters. 
Applying all five control actions considered for CL 5 results in an 26 % reduction considering the simulation 
period with start date of 2003-11-03, which is nearly half the reduction achieved considering the simulation 
period 2003-12-10 (49%) and 2003-08-10 (42%). The simulation period showing the lowest RTC potential is 
the period with the significantly larger intensity. Considering only the reductions achieved prior to controlling 
the outflow of Tank 6 (CL 5), a total reduction of 41% was achieved. Larger CSO volumes occur from Tank 
6 during this simulation period, than during the remaining two, why controlling the outflow of Tank 6 only 
increases the CSO volumes during this simulation period. 
Therefore, considering only one simulation period might result in a large reduction or increase considering the 
simulation period, but a significantly different result considering the variations of flows and rain events 
occurring throughout an entire year or when considering another period. The total reduction achieved for 2003 
applying the control actions developed and optimised based on the three simulation periods (CL 5) selected is 
31%. The total reduction considering the total CSO volumes from the three simulation periods are 40%, but 
with the reduction of individual simulation periods being respectively 42%, 26% and 49%. 
5.3.3.2 
Control Strategy 2: Minimising discharge to the sensitive Park Creek (Tank 6) 
Control Strategy 2 is developed with the aim of reducing discharge to the most sensitive recipient Park Creek. 
Based on this, the first control action is controlling the outflow of Tank 6 (orifice V6) based on the water level 
in Tank 3. The second control action applied is controlling orifice V6, thus the outflow of Tank 3, based on 
the water level in Tank 1 (located downstream of Tank 3, hence a feedback control loop). The applied control 
order of the remaining storage units is based on the method used for Control Strategy 1, locating with the aim 
of utilising the RTC potential, thus orifice V4, orifice V2 and last orifice V5.  
The optimised and applied control actions making up Control action 2, (controlled orifice and the storage unit 
for which the water level must reach the optimal set point for the orifice to close) are presented in Table 22.

32%, 17% and 30% of the CSO volumes for the simulation periods, 2003-08-10, 2003-11-03 and 2003-12-10, 
and a reduction of 25% considering the entire year 2003.

Control Strategy 2 (CS2) the application order was based on the influence on Tank 6, aiming to reduce the 
CSO volume discharged to the sensitive Park Creek. The CSO volumes from the individual locations are

events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 61 of 88 
 
 
The CSO structures (CSO 7-10) are not affected by the control strategies, thus not included in the evaluation 
of the strategies. None of the strategies applied (CS 1 and CS 2) impact the CSO volumes from Tank 5. This 
is in accordance with the results found for the simulation periods. It is due to the optimal set points determined, 
from which orifice V5 will close when Tank 1 is completely filled with water or when it overflows (the orifice 
V5 closes when the water level in Tank 1 reaches 5 m and 5.2 m, for CS 1 and CS 2 respectively). Applying 
the control strategies and set points found by optimisation will furthermore result in an increase of the CSO 
volumes from Tank 2, Tank 4 and Tank 6, but reductions of 75% and 53% of the CSO volumes from Tank 1 
are achieved for CS 1 and CS 2, respectively.  
The control strategy developed with the aim of reducing the CSO volume from Tank 6 discharged to Park 
Creek (CS 2) does not fulfil the intended aim. Instead, the CSO volume from Tank 6 is increased by 59%. 
Despite of this, Control Strategy 2 aims to decrease the total CSO volume from Tank 3, as found from the 
three selected simulation periods. For Control Strategy 1 (CS 1), the control action for Tank 3 controlled 
according to the water level in Tank 1 is applied prior to the control of Tank 3. This results in larger increases 
of the CSO volume from Tank 6 and Tank 3 than found for CS 2, despite a larger set point of V6 (orifice V6 
closes when Tank 3 reaches 4.92 m for CS 1, and 4.68 m for CS 2). This indicates the control of V3 (closes 
when the water level in Tank 1 exceeds 4.95 m) puts an additional constrain on the already overloaded system.  
Overall, applying the control strategy developed and applied based on the overload of the storage units (CS 1), 
shows a reduction in the discharge to Main River (48%) and for the entire system (31%). This is mainly due 
to the reduction of CSO volume from Tank 1 resulting in an increase of the CSO volumes from the remaining 
parts of the system. The control strategy results in an increase of 68% in the discharge to Park Creek.  
Furthermore, a small reduction (0.2%) of the flooding volume of the city is seen, however it should be noticed 
that the flooding of the city during year 2003 only account for 1.4 m3. For the control strategy developed based 
on reducing CSO volume from one location (CS 2), the total reduction of CSO volumes from the system during 
the year is 25%, 35% reduction in the discharge to Main River, but an increase of 55% to Park Creek and no 
reduction nor increase in the flooding of the city.  
The number of CSO events from the individual locations and in total, using 6 hours without CSO as separator, 
are presented in Figure 34.

The control strategies result in less CSO events (55 and 56, respectively) than occurring for the BC (60 CSO 
events). Overall a high correlation is found between the increasing CSO volumes and increasing amount of 
CSO events. Likewise, less CSO events are determined for the locations where less CSO volumes are 
discharged as a result of the applied control actions.  
The best strategy to apply depends on the objectives. In this case, the best result depends on the preferences of 
minimising the total reduction as much as possible, thus Control Strategy 1 is the best solution, or reducing 
the CSO volume and events from the entire system, but with the smallest negative impact on the sensitive Park 
Creek, thus Control Strategy 2. If the preference is towards protecting Park Creek no matter the potential for 
decreasing the total CSO volume from the system, none of the strategies should be applied, thus the Base Case 
(the current system without RTC) is a better solution.  
Developing and optimising a Control Strategy based on the three simulation periods can only be considered 
useful to some extent when applying the finding and evaluating for an entire year. The total reduction found 
from the simulation periods are comparable with the total reductions found for the entire year (2003). The 
simulation periods selected are chosen based on the maximum CSO volumes and are assumed to correspond 
to the 75% smallest rainfall events throughout the entire year, hence extreme events are not considered. When 
simulating the entire year 2003, extreme events are included which potentially influences the CSO volumes 
significantly, thus same reductions cannot be expected considering the individual locations.  
5.3.4 Comparing objective functions 
In this section, the results of optimising RTC parameters based on different objective functions are 
investigated. The three objective functions defined in the method section (section 5.2.1) are compared:  
1) Minimising the CSOs to the Main River and Park Creek, along with flooding of the city (Objective Function 
1, used above),  
2) Minimising the CSOs to the Main River and Park Creek, along with flooding of the city and minimising the 
frequency of CSO events (Objective Function 2),  
3) Objective function minimising the accumulated CSO volumes (Objective Function 3).  
Objective Function 1 with the applied factors: 
Minimize ∑(VCSOs,T1 ∙1,000 + VCSOs,MR ∙2,000 + VCSO,PC ∙5,000 +Vflood,C ∙6,000) 
Objective Function 2 with the applied factors: 
Minimize ∑((VCSOs,T1 ∙1,000 + VCSOs,MR ∙2,000 + VCSO,PC ∙5,000 +Vflood,C ∙6,000) ∙NOV) 
Objective Function 3: 
Minimize ∑(VCSOs,T1 + VCSOs,MR + VCSO,PC+Vflood,C) 
5.3.4.1 
Results of the objective functions 
Using the three selected simulation periods, the three objective functions presented above are investigated 
using various set points to control the outflow from Tank 4 by closing (setting fraction of 0, thus completely 
closed causing no outflow) or opening (setting of 1, fully open with a maximum throttle flow of 80 L/s) orifice 
V4 according to the water level monitored in Tank 1, located downstream of Tank 4 (feedback control loop).  
 
The results of Objective Function 1-3 using different set points (K) for Tank 1 for which the outflow of Tank 
4 (V4) are controlled, is presented in Figure 35. 50 RTC parameters are simulated for each objective function, 
varying in the range from 0 m to 5.2 m (corresponding to BC), with a higher resolution surrounding the optimal 
parameter.  
==PAGE:76==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 63 of 88 
 
 
 
 
Figure 35: Comparing the three defined objective functions. Objective Function 1 is developed with the aim of minimising the CSOs 
to the Main River and Park Creek, along with flooding of the city. Objective Function 2 has the aim of minimising the CSOs to the 
Main River and Park Creek, along with flooding of the city and minimising the frequency of CSO events. Lastly, Objective Function 
3 is optimised with the aim of minimising the accumulated CSO volumes.  
The applied optimiser aims to minimise the objective function as much as possible, thus the optimal set point 
is the one resulting in the smallest outcome of the objective function. The optimal set point to control orifice 
V4 based on Tank 1 is 4.92 m when considering the discharge point, thus aiming to minimise the CSO 
discharge to Park Creek (Objective Function 1). Objective Function 3 relies only on minimising the CSO 
volume, independent of location and frequency of CSO events. The result of the objective function is very 
similar to the results found for Objective Function 1. This implies that, despite the factor considered for the 
CSOs occurring near the WWTP (from Tank 1) is the lowest (fT1 = 1000) applied for Objective Function 1, 
the CSO volumes from this location make up a significantly part of the total CSO volumes considering the 
entire system (83 %, 66 % and 88 % considering the BC of the three simulation period, and 80 % considering 
the total CSO volumes from the three investigated simulation periods, see Figure 27).  
 
For Objective function 2, the optimal set points (K) is 0.2 m, hence nearly 0 m, meaning the optimal control is 
keeping the orifice V4 closed at all times, thus no outflow from Tank 4 to the remaining system. Comparing 
Objective Function 1 and 2 shows, that this optimal value is only due to the CSO events continues for an 
excessive time period. The number of CSO events (NOV) is determined based on the CSOs occurring from the 
entire system. Therefore, in case of an CSO occurrence from Tank 1 ending at 2:00 AM and next CSO from 
the same storage unit begins at 11:00 AM (more than 6 hours later), this would account for two CSO events 
from Tank 1. But since the CSO events considered for Objective Function 2 takes all CSO events from the 
entire system into account, a CSO event occurring from Tank 3 starting at 7:00 AM, 5 hours after the first CSO 
event from Tank 1, the three independent CSO occurrences are considered one CSO event. Thereby, 
multiplying the number of CSO events occurring from the entire system, as it is done for Objective Function 
2, results in a bias towards continuous CSOs from the system during the minimization. The exceedance depths 
of the controllable storage units (Tank 2-6) (considering the size of the sub-catchment relative to the storage 
unit volumes) is of approximately of same size. The set point indicates the water level to be monitored for an 
orifice to close and thereby limit the outflow of the storage unit related to the orifice, thus a set point of low 
order result in more water from the related sub-catchment being stored in the storage unit, than for a set point 
of high order. Furthermore, the control is performed with the aim of emptying the storage units and let water

In the following section, the optimal set points for the controllable storage units (Tank 2-6) is determined using 
Objective Function 1 and 2.  
 
5.3.4.2 
Optimal RTC parameters 
In this section, optimal control set points are determined for all controllable storage units in the system (Tank 
2-5) using Objective Function 1 and Objective Function 2. Optimising the set points by minimising the CSO 
volumes to the Main River and Park Creek, along with flooding of the city, and additionally minimising the 
frequency of CSO events, using 6 hours as separator, for Objective Function 2. Objective Function 3, 
minimising the overall CSO volume, is not considered due to the large similarity to Objective Function 1. The 
RTC strategies considered is the strategies (CS 1 and CS 2) defined in section 0: For Control Strategy 1, the 
order of applying and optimising the control points is based on RTC potential, starting with the maximum RTC 
potential (least CSO volume, thus Tank 4). For Control Strategy 2, the strategy is to minimise the discharge to 
the sensitive Park Creek (discharge from Tank 6), thus starting by controlling Tank 6, the downstream Tank 3 
and then considering the RTC potential for the three remaining units to be controlled.  
The investigated controls scenarios are named based on the Control Strategy i and the objective function j (CS 
i.j). The real time control parameters are optimised using the three simulation periods, but the evaluation is 
again based on the CSO and flooding volumes during entire year 2003.  
Since every actuator (orifice) controlled is regulated based on a sensor located in a downstream storage, the 
control is based on a feedback control loop. The optimised and applied control scenarios developed based on 
two different control strategies (CS 1 and CS 2) and using two different objective functions (Objective 
Function 1 and 2) is presented in Table 23. 
Table 23: The control scenarios are optimised using the Nelder-Mead method, keeping any control action applied prior to the orifice 
controlled constant. The 5 control actions make up Control Strategy i and the Objective Function j (CS i.j).

Out of the 39 identified simulation periods for the year 2003 where CSO occurs for the BC, CSOs only occurs 
from the CSO structures during two periods, thus no reductions of the CSO volumes is achieved from the 
applied control scenarios. For the remaining 37 simulation periods with CSOs, the reductions achieved are in 
the range 8.3-100 %, 1.2-100 %, 5.9-100 % and 5.2-100 % for control scenario CS 1.1, CS 1.2, CS 2.1 and CS 
2.2, respectively. Controlling the system based on the control scenarios, a 100 % reduction is achieved for 3, 
5, 2 and 10 of the simulation periods defined for the year 2003. The average reductions achieved are 56 %, 48 
% 46 % and 53 %, for the control scenarios CS 1.1, CS 1.2, CS 2.1 and CS 2.2, respectively.  
The optimal set points developed and used in the control scenarios are based on optimisation using three 
simulation periods (2003-08-10, 2003-11-03 and 2003-12-10). The three selection points are chosen based on 
the maximum rainfall depth occurring 30 minutes prior for the CSO occurrences within the simulation period. 
The maximum precipitation depths for the three selected simulation periods corresponds to the 50 % - 75 % 
smallest depths for the simulation periods defined for 2003, the group is denoted Q3 (see section 5.3.1). The 
reductions achieved during the simulation periods within group Q3, controlling the system based on the four 
control scenarios are presented in Figure 38. The reduction achieved for the three simulation periods used for 
optimisation and considering the entire group (Q3 tot) is shown above the related bar.  
 
Figure 38: The reductions of the total CSO volumes [%] occuring within the simulation periods for groups Q3. The reductions are 
achieved by applying the four control scenarios (CS 1.1, CS 1.2, CS 2.1 and CS 2.2) and is based on the CSO volumes determine for 
the simultion periods for the Base Case. The total reductions achived for the three simulation periods for which the control actions are 
developed is written above the bars.  
The simulation periods presented above is among the largest CSO volumes occurring for the year 2003, 
considering the strong correlation between CSO volumes and maximum rainfall depths. Based on this, the 
reductions achieved are not expected to be the highest for the entire year. RTC is considered most efficient for 
smaller rainfall events. For all four control scenarios (CS 1.1, CS 1.2, CS 2.1 and CS 2.2), the reductions 
achieved during the three selected simulation periods are below the average reductions achieved considering 
the reductions of the individual simulation periods within Q3 and considering the entire group of Q3 (total). 
For example, the reductions achieved during the three selected simulation periods for control scenario CS 1.1 
is 28 %, 43 % and 49 %, respectively. The reductions achieved considering the individual simulation periods 
within Q3 being in the range of 28-91%, with an average reduction of the CSO volumes of 50 %. For the entire 
group, the total reduction achieved is 46 %. Comparing these results with the average reduction achieved 
considering all simulation periods for year 2003 of 55.6 %, the three selected simulation periods underestimates

The reductions and increasing CSO volumes from the individual CSO locations, discharged to Main River

Figure 39: Reductions achieved [%] for the different locations compared to CSO volume discharged for the Base Case (BC) when 
applying the four control scenarios (CS 1.1, CS 1.2, CS 2.1 and CS 2.2). The locations overflowed during the year 2003, includes a 
manhole (J4), 6 storage units (Tank 1-6), Main River (MR), Park Creek (PC) and the city (C). 
Considering the objective function taking CSO events into account (CS 1.2 and CS 2.2), larger reductions are 
achieved for Tank 1 (reductions >80%) than considering Objective Function 1. For BC, Tank 1 is the location 
causing the largest CSO volumes, thus the significant reductions achieved results in CSO volumes of 
approximately 30,000 and 32,000 m3 for CS 1.2 and CS 2.2, respectively (185,000 m3 for BC). This reduction 
results in a significant change in the distribution of CSO volumes among the locations compared to the BC. 
For the BC, Tank 1 is the main CSO contributor (72 %), while the CSO volume from Tank 1 accounts for 15 
% and 17 % for CS 1.2 and CS 2.2, respectively. Considering the CSO events along with Control Strategy 1 
(considering the RTC potential) the controls applied results in Tank 3 (23 %), Tank 6 (22 %) and Tank 5 (20 
%) causing larger CSO volumes than Tank 1 (15 %).  The large difference between the locations causing the 
main CSO volumes for Objective Function 1 and 2, implies that the control strategy does not impact the 
distribution as much as the consideration of CSO events (Objective Function 2). A less significant reduction 
is achieved for the flooding of the city (0.2%), corresponding to reduction achieved using Control Strategy 1, 
but not considering the objective function not taken CSO events into account. Despite of the improvement 
achieved for Tank 1 and manhole J4, considering the CSO event frequency, the CSO volumes from the 
remaining parts of the system is not improved significantly considering the CSO events.  
The reductions achieved considering the entire year 2003, is 31 %, 21 %, 25 % and 24 % for control scenario 
CS 1.1, CS 1.2, CS 2.1 and CS 2.2, respectively. The reductions achieved for the three selected simulation 
periods used for optimisation of the control parameters are 28-49 %, 20-33 %, 18-32 % and 14-32 %, thus 
some of the simulation periods overestimates the reductions while other underestimates compared to the 
reductions achieved considering the entire year. This implies that optimising based on selected simulation
