The number of CSO events, using 6 hours without CSO as separator, considering the overflow locations and 
as a total discharged to the receiving water bodies (Main River and Park Creek) (considered as NOV for 
Objective Function 2) are presented in Figure 40.  
 
Figure 40: CSO events identified from the individual locations and total CSO events using 6 hours as separator, for the Base Case 
(BC) and the five control scenarios (CS 1.1, CS 1.2, CS 2.1 and CS 2.2). Two events from two locations occurring simultaneously or 
within 6 hours is considered one event for the total determination. 
By minimizing objective Function 1 and 2, the aim is to minimize the overall CSO volumes, but an additional 
factor is applied to the CSO volumes discharged to Park Creek (CSOs from Tank 6 is ‘penalized’ with a factor 
of 5,000) and the city (J4 flooding volume with a factor of 6,000), thus expected to minimize the CSOs from 
these locations prior to the remaining locations for which the factors applied are lower (a factor of 2,000 is 
added to CSO volumes from Tank 2-5 and a factor of 1,000 is applied to CSOs from Tank 1 due to the location 
near the WWTP). Due to the significantly larger CSO volumes from Tank 1 compared to the other locations, 
minimizing the objective functions, independent of the strategies applied, are found to minimizing the CSO 
volume from Tank 1 despite of the applied factors. Furthermore, the large CSO volumes from Tank 1 is 
discharged to Main River during significantly more CSO events than occurring from the reaming system, 
despite of Tank 1 not being the overall main contributor. 
The total CSO events are based on CSOs from the entire system by neglecting the different locations and only 
taking the 6 hours separator into account. Comparing the CSO events from the individual locations with the 
total number of CSO events occurring, implies that the majority of the CSO events from the different locations 
occurs simultaneously, resulting in less total CSO events than the sum of all events from the individual 
locations.  
Considering the objective function taken the total number of CSO events into account (Objective Function 2), 
less CSO events are determined compared to the number obtained for Objective Function 1, only considering 
the CSO volumes, despite of the smaller reductions achieved for Objective Function 2. This implies one of 
two things: either the storm- and wastewater in the system is more equally distributed when controlling using 
parameters developed taking CSO events into account, thus resulting in the CSOs occurring simultaneously. 
Or it implies that the CSO events occurs for longer time periods, thus the 6 hours without overflow are found 
more frequently considering Objective Function 1 than 2. This is investigated and the results of the total CSO 
volume, number of total CSO events and the total duration for which CSO occurs (accumulated) are presented 
in Table 24.

It is seen that the reduction of CSO events determined for the optimisation performed taken the CSO events 
into account (Objective Function 2) control scenario CS 1.2 and CS 2.2, compared to not including the CSO 
events in the optimisation (CS 1.1 and CS 2.1), results in larger CSO volumes and longer durations. This 
implies that the CSOs does not just occur simultaneous throughout the system but is discharged to the receiving 
water bodies over longer time periods. Thereby, the selection of the best control solution is not just a matter of 
preferring to reduce CSO events, but the increased CSO volumes and durations must be considered. Whether 
the water overflowing occurs from same rainfall events could influence the pollutant discharged. If two CSO 
events occur from same rain event, the first CSO occurrence is assumed to contain higher concentration of the 
pollutants, thus a second CSO occurrence might not be as unwanted if this results in another first flush event 
not occurring. The preferences towards same CSO volume being discharged during one instead of two separate 
events is due to the higher concentration of pollutants (TSS, COD, ammonia, etc.) related to the first flush. 
Another factor to be considered is dilution. If the CSOs occurs from same rain event, the larger volumes being 
discharge might just have a positive impact on the dilution but could potentially cause overload of the treatment 
located at the CSO discharge, thus reduced treatment of the water discharged to the receiving water bodies.  
The strategy preferred might depend on local restrictions of how often CSO events are allowed to occur. In 
this case, the concentrations of pollutants discharged to the receiving water bodies are unknown, thus the CSO 
frequency could potentially be of high importance in the decision making regarding the best strategy to apply. 
Furthermore, discharge of untreated water from combined sewer systems contains bacteria (e.g. e-coli) and 
generally higher concentration of the pollutants than untreated water from a rain pipes in a separate system, 
thus the CSO frequency are of higher importance considering a combined sewer system. If the receiving water 
bodies are bathing water, no CSOs are allowed if used for bathing purposes, thus the strategy reducing the 
CSO frequency but causing larger volumes during a CSO event is preferred over strategies causing more events 
but reducing volumes.  
Furthermore, strong correlations appear for the total CSO volumes from a location and the number of CSO 
events, thus larger CSO volumes is assumed to occur during more CSO events independent of strategy or 
objective function applied. 
5.3.5 Reductions (comparing with literature)  
In the following section, the results of the applied control scenarios CS 1.1, CS 1.2, CS 2.1 and CS 2.2 are 
compared to an RTC control strategy based on Equal Filling Degree (EFD). The control rules used for the 
Equal Filling Degree (EFD) control strategy is developed by (Schütze et al. 2017) and implemented in SWMM 
by (Sun et al., n.d.). For the EFD control strategy, four controllable storage units (Tank 2, Tank 3, Tank 4 and 
Tank 6) in the system are controlled. Tank 5 is defined as a controllable storage unit (unlike Tank 1) but is not 
controlled in the EFD strategy. The optimal control of the throttle outflow from Tank 5 defined for the control 
strategies CS 1.1, CS 2.1 and CS 2.2 (set points ≥ 5 m, see section 5.3.4.2), along with the increasing CSO 
volumes discharged from Tank 5 when controlled (CS 1.2) emphasizes the storage unit Tank 5 being 
overloaded, thus having a low RTC potential.  
Besides the Base Case (BC), the five control strategies are compared to the static control with throttle controls 
(SC) defined by (Schütze et al. 2017) and (Sun et al., n.d.). The total CSO volumes and the distribution among 
the locations are presented in Figure 41.  
==PAGE:84==

and an Equal Filling Degree strategy (both described in literature ), along with applying all five control actions defined in this thesis 
(CS 1.1, CS 1.2, CS 2.1 and CS 2.2), for simulations conducted for the entire year of 2003. 
The static throttle flows from the storage units in the SC control scenario are lower than the throttle flows used 
in the defined BC. For every scenario presented, the total CSO volumes are less than the total CSO volume 
discharged for the BC. Neither the SC nor the EFD control strategies results in larger reductions of the total 
CSO volumes than achieved for the remaining four control strategies.

Comparing the results of the static (SC) control (16 % reduction of the total CSO volume) and the control 
strategies applying RTC, larger reductions, thus smaller CSO volumes occurs, from the use of RTC (22-31 %). 
All control scenarios applied, results in a decrease of the CSO volume discharged to Main River and an increase 
of the CSO volumes discharged to the sensitive Park Creek, with the smallest CSO volume discharged using 
the EFD control strategy. The control strategy of applying control actions and optimise these based on either 
the RTC potential (CS 1.1 and CS 1.2) or reduce CSOs from a certain location (CS 2.1 and CS 2.2), results in 
higher total reductions, but requires a certain knowledge of the system, but taken the reductions achieved into 
account might be consider a better strategy than EFD. Developing an EFD control strategy for a specific control 
system likewise considers a certain knowledge of the system and more control rules should be considered. The 
methodology of the EFD strategy is well known and described in the literature, thus might be easier to project 
upon other RTC systems, despite of the control rules being highly dependent on the system applied for.   
To decide for the best control solution of the worked example ‘Astlingen’, the concentrations should be 
considered. More CSO events might result in more pollutions discharged to the receiving water bodies – 
whereas more events within the same rain event might not influence the pollution discharged highly due to the 
main pollution found from first flush and the potential treatment located at the overflow locations will further 
influence the concentration of the pollutions discharged, thus the  impacts on the water bodied. 
The problematics related to the increasing CSO volumes discharged to Park Creek can potentially be solved 
by implementing more treatment in Tank 6, thus decreasing the concentration of the pollution discharged 
without necessarily decreasing the CSO volume from the storage unit.

Considering the CSO volumes occurring from the respective years during the defined simulation, the year 
2003 was found representative for remaining years. The CSO volume determined for the entire year of 2003 
(255,599 m3) is the second lowest CSO volume discharged considering the yearly discharges, thus below the 
median of 310,638 m3.  
The median of the reduction of CSO volumes achieved throughout the years is 29 %, 21 %, 23 % and 23 % 
for control scenario CS 1.1, CS 1.2, CS 2.1 and CS 2.2, respectively. The reductions achieved during the 
selected year of 2003 is 31 %, 21 %, 25 % and 24 %, thus close to the median reductions, despite of the 
significantly smaller CSO volumes for year 2003 than the remaining years. Regardless of the control 
parameters being developed and optimised based on simulation periods occurring during 2003, higher 
reductions are achieved applying all four control scenarios for the years 2000, 2006, 2007 and 2009, than 
achieved for 2003. Common for the years with reductions similar to the selected year, is that the CSO volumes 
from the BC is among the lowest, considering all year. 
Based on the defined simulation periods, it is investigated whether any correlation exists between the 
maximum CSO volume for each year and the representativeness of the selected year. This is investigated by 
comparing the magnitude of the maximum CSO for the years with the similarity of the reductions achieved for 
the individual years with the reductions achieved for the assumed representative year 2003.  
The maximum CSO volume per year occurring within a defined simulation period is presented in Figure 44. 
The years where the reduction of the CSO volume exceeds or equal to the reductions achieved for year 2003, 
the selected year used for the optimisation, is highlighted with red and white stripes.    
==PAGE:88==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 75 of 88 
 
 
 
Figure 44: The maximum CSO volume with the simulation periods for each year. The years with reduction of the CSO volumes higher 
or equal to the reductions achieved for the representative year of 2003 is highlighted with red and white stripes.    
A strong correlation is found between the magnitude of the total CSO volume for the BC and the magnitude 
of the maximum CSO volume within a simulation period. Consider the individual years, he largest total CSO 
volume for the BC and the maximum CSO volume within a simulation period is found for year 2008. The 
reductions of the CSO volumes achieved for the years 2001, 2002, 2004 and 2005 applying control scenario 
CS 1.1, is the same reduction as achieved for year 2003. The reductions achieved for the remaining three 
control scenarios (CS 1.2, CS 2.1 and CS 2.2) are lower than achieved for year 2003, but due to the magnitude 
of the reductions, not considered significantly lower. The largest differences between the reductions achieved 
is found for year 2008, where the reductions are lower for all four control scenarios compared to the reductions 
achieved for year 2003. The total CSO volume for year 2008 is the largest defined and the maximum CSO 
volume occurring within a simulation period is significantly larger for year 2008 than the remaining years 
which could imply an extreme rain event occurring for the year. RTC is considered most beneficial for smaller 
rain events, thus the applied strategies are not expected to be beneficial during extreme events.  
For all years, reductions of the CSO volumes similar to those achieved using the selected year of 2003 is 
achieved even though the control parameters for the applied control strategies is developed and optimised 
based on one year (2003). The magnitude of the reductions achieved varies from slightly larger to smaller than 
the selected year. Based on this, the optimisations performed for simulation periods defined for year 2003 is 
considered representative for the entire dataset.

6 Limitations and recommendations for further work 
- 
Validation of the methods: The methods developed in this project, are only investigated based on the 
‘Astlingen’ benchmark model for which they are developed. To validate the high computationally 
estimation efficiency obtained by the thoroughly selection of rainfall events for use in the estimation 
of RTC parameters, the methods should be validated based on models, potentially containing larger 
catchments.  
 
- 
Choosing one representative year: From the analysis performed in order to select one representative 
year, other years than the one selected (2003) have equally high potential. If other parameters were 
including e.g. conducting the t-test based on the variance instead of the mean, the estimate of RTC 
parameter could have been conducted using another year. Even though the year 2003 is considered 
representative for the reaming years, variations between the reductions achieved for the year 2003 and 
2008 are present. Other analyses and selection of one representative year could be investigated further 
to simplify and optimise the selection method.  
 
- 
Rainfall events causing CSOs: A constant of 30 minutes prior to a CSO occurrence was chosen for 
the estimation of rainfall events causing CSOs. This assumption has a risk of not considering all 
rainfall events filling the storage units, but only considers the final rainfall events causing the CSO. 
For further investigations one could consider constructing a method to determine the exact rainfall 
events causing CSOs from an urban drainage system, while considering the dry flow.  
 
- 
The optimisation tool: The estimation of RTC parameters was based on the ‘Nelder-Mead simplex 
algorithm’. To decrease the computation time related to the optimisation, the tool was limited to 
optimise set points in the range 3-5 m. Before applying this limitation, it was ensured that the optimal 
set point was found within this range, and that the assumption could be applied to all storage units. If 
the Python-script made is applied to other catchments, the limitation needs to be investigated or 
removed. To increase the computationally estimation efficient and limit the increases related to this 
method, another optimisation tool could be used instead of the ‘Nelder-Mead simplex algorithm’. 
 
 
==PAGE:90==
Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating on selecting 
events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 77 of 88 
 
 
7 
CONCLUSION  
From the 10 years long-term rain series available for the Astlingen’ benchmark model, simulation periods were 
identified based on a rough estimate of rainfall events potentially causing CSOs from the storage. For each 
storage unit, the events were determined according to the maximum throttle flow and/or the storage capacity, 
all considered within a certain duration(s). Based on this, 647 simulation periods (covering 44 % of the 10 
years available rain series) was identified to pose a risk of CSOs.   
Divided into years, the simulation periods have been investigated. The simulation periods identified for the 
year 2003 was considered most representative for the remaining nine years, considering a yearly distribution 
and combined (2000-2009). For year 2003, the rainfall events within the simulation periods, were in high 
accordance with the remaining years, without significant extremes potentially influencing the estimation of 
RTC parameters. Same representativeness was determined for the CSOs occurrences and events, including the 
quantity (CSOs from 63% of the 63 identified simulation periods), frequency (167 events) and volume (total 
CSO volume of 301,868 m3). A t-test was performed, indicating that no significant differences was found 
between the mean CSO volumes determined for simulations conducted using the individual years and the 
remaining years. For the identification of rainfall events causing CSOs, rainfall events occurring 30 minutes 
prior to a CSO occurrence was determined a sufficient estimate. 
The estimation of Real Time Control parameters was based on simulation periods identified for year 2003. In 
conclusion, RTC parameters estimated based on rain events with larger depths (7.4 – 10.6 mm), are considered 
most representative regarding the reduction of the CSO volumes achieved. This is based on the comparison 
between the reductions achieved for the simulation periods used in the estimates (14 %), and the reductions 
achieved considering the entire time period available for the historical long-term rain series (9 %). From the 
representative group, three simulation periods with the largest CSO volumes, were found very representative, 
with a significantly higher computational efficiency, thus used for any further estimations of RTC parameters. 
The application of two control strategies – one based on RTC potential and the other with the aim of reducing 
CSOs to the most sensitive discharge point – show similar reductions of the total CSO volumes and events 
from the urban drainage system. The main differences between these, are the locations where reductions and 
increases occur. Objective functions considering the CSO frequency and/or the sensitivity of discharge points, 
has a high impact on the RTC parameters estimated and thereby the CSO volumes and events. The reduction 
is larger when the objective function does not include the frequency of events. RTC parameters estimates for 
the two objective functions, result in a decrease in CSO events, with the largest reduction achieved for the 
objective function considering CSO event frequency. Despite this, the total CSO duration is longer when 
aiming to minimise the number of CSO events. 
 
Any applied control to the urban drainage system, result in both increases and reductions of the CSO volumes 
and events at different locations in the system (storage units, CSO structures and manholes). Reductions of the 
total CSO volumes (24-31 %) and events (7-32 %) are achieved overall for the urban drainage system. The 
main reductions of CSO volumes (53-78 %) are seen for the storage tank most frequently overflown and with 
the largest CSO volumes. 
 
The CSO volumes of applying the RTC parameters, strategies and objective functions developed for this 
project, have been compared to two strategies found in the literature, developed for the ‘Astlingen’ benchmark 
model used in this project. One strategy is based on Equal Filling Degree and the other applying static flow 
controls. Larger reductions of the total CSO volumes from the urban drainage system, are achieved when 
applying strategies with RTC (22-31 %) instead of static control (16 %). The total CSO volume discharged to 
the receiving water body considered the most sensitive, are lower considering the literature found strategies. 
However, result in larger total CSO volumes to the entire urban drainage system.  
 
In the literature, controlling a system with RTC is found most beneficial for larger catchments. The catchment 
of the ‘Astlingen’ benchmark model, used for this project, is compared to the literature considered a small 
catchment. Therefore, larger reductions might occur when applying the controls developed here to larger 
catchments.

B. Pyhon script for selecting rainfall event 
The following 14 pages contains the Python script (‘SelectingRain’) developed to selected rainfall events 
exceeding the depths specified for the individual storage unit and the simulation periods identified based on 
these. Lastly, it contains the code used for identification of rainfall events within the simulation periods.

#!! Rain time steps interval
ts = 5 #[min]
#!! Minutes to be considered BEFORE the event starts
T_before = 8*60
#!! Minutes to be considered without rain AFTER the event ends (dry period)
T_AfterDry = 24*60
hr = 60/ts #Time steps per hour
T_bf = round(T_before/ts) #Dry period before event start [time steps]
T_Dry = round(T_AfterDry/ts) #Dry period after event ends [time steps]
1
==PAGE:97==
    
    #### Controllable storage units: Choose events based on the depth of the storage unit
    #to enter: Rain series, Duration [min], Volume of storage unit [m3] and Impervious area [ha]
def Evn_Control(Rain,T_dur,SU_Vol,A_imperv):
    T_s = round(T_dur / ts)    #Duration converted from min to timesteps   
    R_s = round((SU_Vol / (A_imperv*10000))*1000,3)    #Rain depths to consider
      
    #Determine the sum for timesteps corresponding to the duration time
    Rain['Rain_sum']=Rain['Rain'].rolling(T_s).sum()
    Rain=Rain.where(Rain > 1e-6 , other=0) #Set values below to zero (sum of zeros is 7.49e-15)
    
    #Find indicies for all timesteps where the rolling sum of the rain depth exceeds the basin depth 
    Events_thres = [i for i, e in enumerate(Rain.Rain_sum) if e >= R_s]
    Events_thres.sort()
    
    #Determine each event by deleting overlaps (1 timestep between)
    Evn=[i for i, x in enumerate(np.diff(Events_thres)>1) if x]
    Evn=[Events_thres[Evn[p]+1] for p in range(0,len(Evn))]
    Evn.append(Events_thres[0])
    Evn.sort()
    
    #Find start of each event (by finding first zero occuring before the event)
    StartEvn_idx=[]
    for p in range(0,len(Evn)):
        StartEvn_idx.append((len(Rain.Rain[Evn[p]::-1])
        - list(Rain.Rain[Evn[p]::-1]).index(0)-1)+1)
        
    #########                Time before simulation starts 
    H_bfEvn=[]
    
    #Identify index of chosen period before event start - ensures T_bf is not in the middle of an event
    for p in range(0,len(StartEvn_idx)):
        if StartEvn_idx[p]<T_bf:
            Hbf=0
        else:
            Hbf = (len(Rain.Rain[round(StartEvn_idx[p]-T_bf)::-1])
            - list(Rain.Rain[round(StartEvn_idx[p]-T_bf)::-1]).index(0)-1)
        H_bfEvn.append(Hbf)
            
        
    #Rolling sum of chosen hours before event starts (used to define dry periods)
2
==PAGE:98==
    RolSum_Hbf = [sum(Rain.Rain[H_bfEvn[p]:StartEvn_idx[p]]) for p in range(0,len(StartEvn_idx))] 
    
    #Indicies for events where the chosen period before the event is a dry period
    replc_idx=[i for i, e in enumerate(RolSum_Hbf) if e == 0]
    
    #List of indicies for when the simulations should start
    Start_R = H_bfEvn.copy()
    
     #For all dry periods (replc_idx) the values in the list should be replaced with Start_idx
    Start_idx1=[StartEvn_idx[replc_idx[p]] for p in range(0,len(replc_idx))]
    for (index, replacement) in zip(replc_idx, Start_idx1):
        Start_R[index] = replacement
    Start_R.sort()
    
        
    #########                End time of simulation - DRY period
    #Identify end of event (nearest zero after event)
    End_idx = []
    
    Rain['Rol_2'] = Rain['Rain'].rolling(2).sum()
    Rain.loc[Rain.Rol_2<1e-6,'Rol_2']=0 #
    
    for p in range(0,len(StartEvn_idx)):
        End_idx.append((Rain.Rol_2[StartEvn_idx[p]:]==0).idxmax(axis=1,skipna=True))
        
    
    #Rolling sum rain in period after events ends (used to define dry periods)
    Rain['RolSum_Haft']=Rain['Rain'].rolling(T_Dry).sum()
    Rain.loc[Rain.RolSum_Haft<1e-6,'RolSum_Haft']=0 #
    
    #Indicies for dry period corresponding to each event simulation  (Dry period has occured for more than T_Dry) 
    Dry_End=[]
    
    #Identify index of chosen period after event end - ensures T_Dry is not in the middle of an event
    for p in range(0,len(End_idx)):
        Evn_period=Rain.RolSum_Haft[End_idx[p]+T_Dry:-1] #Investegated period starts when events starts
        Dry_End.append(min(Evn_period[Evn_period < 1].index)) #Find index of first dry period after the events
    
    #Search for duplicates in end date
    Sim_Rain_idx = tuple(zip(Start_R,StartEvn_idx,End_idx,Dry_End))
    visited = set() 
3
==PAGE:99==
    Output = []   
    
    # Iteration searching for dubplicates
    for a, b, c, d in Sim_Rain_idx: 
        if not d in visited: 
            visited.add(d) 
            Output.append((a, b, c, d)) 
    
    Start_idx, StartEvn_idx, End_idx, DryEnd_idx = zip(*Output)
    Start_idx=list(Start_idx)
    StartEvn_idx=list(StartEvn_idx)
    End_idx = list(End_idx)
    DryEnd_idx = list(DryEnd_idx)
    
    #Dataframe containing datetime and index no. for start of events
    Start_R = Rain.iloc[Start_idx,[0,1]]
    Start_R['Datetime'] = Start_R['Date'] + " " + Start_R['Time']
    Start_R['Datetime'] = pd.to_datetime(Start_R['Datetime'], format = '%m/%d/%Y %H:%M:%S')
    Start_R=Start_R.drop(columns=['Date', 'Time'])
    Start_R['idx']=Start_R.index
    Start_R=Start_R.reset_index(drop=True)
    
    #Dataframe containing datetime and index no. for dry end of events
    EndDry_Rain = Rain.iloc[DryEnd_idx,[0,1]]
    EndDry_Rain['Datetime'] = EndDry_Rain['Date'] + " " + EndDry_Rain['Time']
    EndDry_Rain['Datetime'] = pd.to_datetime(EndDry_Rain['Datetime'], format = '%m/%d/%Y %H:%M:%S')
    EndDry_Rain=EndDry_Rain.drop(columns=['Date', 'Time'])
    EndDry_Rain['idx']=EndDry_Rain.index
      
    
    #Lists and DataFrame of start time and end time, respectively
    RainSim_Start=list(Start_R.iloc[:,0])
    RainSim_DryEnd = list(EndDry_Rain.iloc[:,0])
    RainSim = pd.DataFrame({'Start':RainSim_Start, 'End':RainSim_DryEnd})
    RainSim=RainSim.loc[RainSim.End-RainSim.Start>timedelta(days=0,hours=0,minutes=0)] #Remove periods where start and en
    RainSim_idx = pd.DataFrame({'Start_idx':Start_idx, 'DryEnd_idx':DryEnd_idx, 'EvnStart_idx':StartEvn_idx, 'EvnEnd_idx'
    return RainSim, RainSim_idx
#Non-controllable sotorage units: Choose events exceeding the throttle volume and storage unit volume
4
==PAGE:100==
        #Enter: Rain series, throttle flow [m3/s], Impervious area [ha], Volume of storage unit [m3] 
        #and list of Durations [min]
        #!! If less than four durations criteria set unused values to >10^8
def Evn_NonControl(Rain, Thr_Q, A_imperv, SU_Vol, T_dur):
    T_s = [round(x / ts) for x in T_dur] #Duration [time steps]
    
    
    #Determine the sum for timesteps corresponding to the duration time
    Rain['Rain_s1']=Rain['Rain'].rolling(T_s[0]).sum()
    Rain['Rain_s2']=Rain['Rain'].rolling(T_s[1]).sum()
    Rain['Rain_s3']=Rain['Rain'].rolling(T_s[2]).sum()
    Rain['Rain_s4']=Rain['Rain'].rolling(T_s[3]).sum()
    Rain=Rain.where(Rain > 1e-6 , other=0) #Set values below to zero (sum of zeros is 7.49e-15)
    
    A_imp = round(A_imperv*10000) # Impervious area [m2]
    Thr_Vol = [(Thr_Q*60 * x) for x in T_s] #Throttle volume after chosen durations [m3] 
    Thr_Out = [((x / (A_imp))*1000) for x in Thr_Vol] # Throttle outflow [mm]
    SU_depth = ((SU_Vol/A_imp)*1000) #Storage unit depth w. respect to upstream catch [mm]
    Tot_depth = [(x + SU_depth) for x in Thr_Out] #Total depth, the rain depth must exceed [mm]
    
    ##Find indicies for all timesteps where the rolling sum exceeds any of the criteria
    Events_thres = list(Rain[(Rain['Rain_s1'] >= Tot_depth[0])| (Rain['Rain_s2'] >= Tot_depth[1]) |
            (Rain['Rain_s3'] >= Tot_depth[2])|(Rain['Rain_s4'] >= Tot_depth[3])].index)
    
    Evn=[i for i, x in enumerate(np.diff(Events_thres)>1) if x]
    Evn=[Events_thres[Evn[p]+1] for p in range(0,len(Evn))]
    Evn.append(Events_thres[0])
    Evn.sort()
        
    #Find start of each event (by finding first zero occuring before the event)
    StartEvn_idx=[]
    for p in range(0,len(Evn)):
        StartEvn_idx.append((len(Rain.Rain[Evn[p]::-1])
        - list(Rain.Rain[Evn[p]::-1]).index(0)-1)+1)
        
    
    #########                Time before simulation starts 
    
    H_bfEvn=[]

    for p in range(0,len(StartEvn_idx)):

            Hbf=0
        else:
            Hbf = (len(Rain.Rain[round(StartEvn_idx[p]-T_bf)::-1])
            - list(Rain.Rain[round(StartEvn_idx[p]-T_bf)::-1]).index(0)-1)
        H_bfEvn.append(Hbf)

    replc_idx=[i for i, e in enumerate(RolSum_Hbf) if e == 0]

    Start_R = H_bfEvn.copy()

    Start_idx1=[StartEvn_idx[replc_idx[p]] for p in range(0,len(replc_idx))]
    for (index, replacement) in zip(replc_idx, Start_idx1):
        Start_R[index] = replacement
    Start_R.sort()

    for p in range(0,len(StartEvn_idx)):
        End_idx.append((Rain.Rol_2[StartEvn_idx[p]:]==0).idxmax(axis=1,skipna=True))
        
        
    #Rolling sum rain in period after events ends (used to define dry periods)
    Rain['RolSum_Haft']=Rain['Rain'].rolling(T_Dry).sum()
    Rain.loc[Rain.RolSum_Haft<1e-6,'RolSum_Haft']=0 #
    
    #Indicies for dry period corresponding to each event simulation  (Dry period has occured for more than T_Dry)

    Dry_End=[]
    
    #Identify index of chosen period after event end - ensures T_Dry is not in the middle of an event
    for p in range(0,len(End_idx)):
        Evn_period=Rain.RolSum_Haft[End_idx[p]+T_Dry:-1] #Investegated period starts when events starts
        Cond = Rain[End_idx[p]+T_Dry:-1]
        Dry_End.append(min(Evn_period[Cond.Rain_s4 < 1].index))
        #Find index of first dry period after the events
    
    #Search for duplicates in end date
    Sim_Rain_idx = tuple(zip(Start_R,StartEvn_idx,End_idx,Dry_End))
    visited = set() 
    Output = []   
    
    # Iteration searching for dubplicates
    for a, b, c, d in Sim_Rain_idx: 
        if not d in visited: 
            visited.add(d) 
            Output.append((a, b, c, d)) 
    
    Start_idx, StartEvn_idx, End_idx, DryEnd_idx = zip(*Output)
    Start_idx=list(Start_idx)
    StartEvn_idx=list(StartEvn_idx)

    Start_R['Datetime'] = pd.to_datetime(Start_R['Datetime'], format = '%m/%d/%Y %H:%M:%S')
    Start_R=Start_R.drop(columns=['Date', 'Time'])
    Start_R['idx']=Start_R.index
    Start_R=Start_R.reset_index(drop=True)

    EndDry_Rain = Rain.iloc[DryEnd_idx,[0,1]]
    EndDry_Rain['Datetime'] = EndDry_Rain['Date'] + " " + EndDry_Rain['Time']
    EndDry_Rain['Datetime'] = pd.to_datetime(EndDry_Rain['Datetime'], format = '%m/%d/%Y %H:%M:%S')
    EndDry_Rain=EndDry_Rain.drop(columns=['Date', 'Time'])
    EndDry_Rain['idx']=EndDry_Rain.index

    RainSim_DryEnd = list(EndDry_Rain.iloc[:,0])
    RainSim = pd.DataFrame({'Start':RainSim_Start, 'End':RainSim_DryEnd})
    RainSim=RainSim.loc[RainSim.End-RainSim.Start>timedelta(days=0,hours=0,minutes=0)] #Remove periods where start and en
    RainSim_idx = pd.DataFrame({'Start_idx':Start_idx, 'DryEnd_idx':DryEnd_idx, 'EvnStart_idx':StartEvn_idx, 'EvnEnd_idx'
    return RainSim, RainSim_idx    
##Find (final) events to include - based on more than one rain series
def RemoveOverlap(*Events):
    
#Merge and sort events by start date 
    Events_list = list(Events)
    RainSim_merged1 = pd.concat(Events_list)
    RainSim_merged = RainSim_merged1.sort_values(by=['Start']) 
    RainSim_merged_list=list(zip(RainSim_merged.Start, RainSim_merged.End))
    
    #Compare datetimes two rows at a time
    #If datetimes overlap, keep minimum start date and maximum end date,
    #otherwise keep unsorted data 
    result = []
    Evn_unsort = RainSim_merged_list[0]
    for Evn_sort in RainSim_merged_list[1:]:
        if Evn_unsort[1] >= Evn_sort[0]-timedelta(hours=1): #Merge events with less than 1 hour between:
            Evn_unsort = ((min(Evn_unsort[0], Evn_sort[0]), 
                           max(Evn_unsort[1], Evn_sort[1])))
        else:
            result.append(Evn_unsort)
            Evn_unsort = Evn_sort
    else:
        result.append(Evn_unsort)
    SimRain = [(F[0], F[1]) for F in result]
    RainSim = pd.DataFrame(SimRain, columns =['Start', 'End'])  
        
    return RainSim

#Determine datetime for start and end of event
def Eventno(df_Intervals,df_Evn):
    import datetime
    s = pd.Series(df_Intervals['Event_no'].values, pd.IntervalIndex.from_arrays(df_Intervals['Start']-datetime.timedelta(
                  df_Intervals['End']+datetime.timedelta(minutes=1)))
    df_Evn['Event_no'] = df_Evn['Start'].map(s)
    return df_Evn
#Based on criteria from storage unis - without overlaps

def Events_Period(Erftx_Tx):
    a=Erft1.Rain
#    a = Erftx_Tx.Rain
#    #Find start and end of events with rain
    b = list(map(int,[i != 0 for i in a]))
    idx = []   
    ind = 0    
    for n,i in enumerate(b):
        if (n+1 == len(b)) or (b[n] != b[n+1]):
            idx.append((ind,n))
            ind = n+1
    if a[0]==0:
        idx=idx[1::2]
    
    else: idx=idx[::2]
                           
    EventStart, EventEnd = zip(*idx)
  
    
    ################
    EventCumsum=[]
    EventDur_min=[]
    for p in range(0,len(idx)):
        EventCumsum.append(a[EventStart[p]:EventEnd[p]+1].sum())
        EventDur_min.append(((EventEnd[p]+1)-EventStart[p])*5)
    I_mmHr = [(x / y)*60 for x, y in zip(EventCumsum, EventDur_min)]
        
        
    Erftx_Tx=pd.DataFrame({'EventCumsum':EventCumsum,'EventDur_min':EventDur_min,
                           'I_mmHr':I_mmHr},index=EventEnd)
    
    #5, 10, 15 and 30 min. intensities
    Five_minI=[]
    Five_minIdx=[]
    Ten_minI=[]
    Ten_minIdx=[]
    Fifteen_minI=[]
    Fifteen_minIdx=[]
    Thirty_minI=[]
    Thirty_minIdx=[]
    
11
==PAGE:107==
    #Intensities in mm/hr (for 5, 10, 15 and 30 min)
    for p in range(0,len(idx)):
        h=a[EventStart[p]:EventEnd[p]+1]
        for d in range(1,len(h)+1):
            Five_minI.append(((sum(h[d-1:d]))/5)*60)
            Five_minIdx.append(max(h[d-1:d].index))
        for e in range(2,len(h)+1):
            Ten_minI.append(((sum(h[e-2:e]))/10)*60)
            Ten_minIdx.append(max(h[e-2:e].index))
        for f in range(3,len(h)+1):
            Fifteen_minI.append(((sum(h[f-3:f]))/15)*60)
            Fifteen_minIdx.append(max(h[f-3:f].index))
        for g in range(6,len(h)+1):
            Thirty_minI.append(((sum(h[g-6:g]))/30)*60)
            Thirty_minIdx.append(max(h[g-6:g].index))
            
    Intensity5=pd.DataFrame({'Five_minI':Five_minI},index=Five_minIdx)            
    Intensity10=pd.DataFrame({'Ten_minI':Ten_minI},index=Ten_minIdx)
    Intensity15=pd.DataFrame({'Fifteen_minI':Fifteen_minI},index=Fifteen_minIdx)
    Intensity30=pd.DataFrame({'Thirty_minI':Thirty_minI},index=Thirty_minIdx)
    Erftx_Tx1=pd.concat([Erftx_Tx,Intensity5,Intensity10,Intensity15,
               Intensity30], axis=1, sort=False)
    return Erftx_Tx1
    
Erft1_events=Events_Period(Erft1)
Erft1 = pd.concat([Erft1,Erft1_events], axis=1, sort=False)
Erft2_events=Events_Period(Erft2)
Erft2 = pd.concat([Erft2,Erft2_events], axis=1, sort=False)
Erft3_events=Events_Period(Erft3)
Erft3 = pd.concat([Erft3,Erft3_events], axis=1, sort=False)

Erft4 = pd.concat([Erft4,Erft4_events], axis=1, sort=False)

################################# DEFINE SIM PERIOD #######################
def SimPeriod(Erftx,SimPer):
    Erftx_Sim = []
    for p in range(0,len(SimPer)):
        Period=Erftx[(Erftx.Datetime>=SimPer.Start[p]) & (Erftx.Datetime <= SimPer.End[p])]
        Period = Period.set_index('Datetime')
        Erftx_Sim.append(Period)
        
    return Erftx_Sim
Erft1_Sim = SimPeriod(Erft1,SimPeriods)
Erft2_Sim = SimPeriod(Erft2,SimPeriods)
Erft3_Sim = SimPeriod(Erft3,SimPeriods)
Erft4_Sim = SimPeriod(Erft4,SimPeriods)
Erft2_T1=SimPeriod(Erft2,Tank1_n)
Erft3_T2=SimPeriod(Erft3,Tank2_n)

Erft2_T6=SimPeriod(Erft2,Tank6_n)

#Concat data from Tank_n for same events
def ConcatEvents(Tank_n,Sim_Events,Erftx_T):
    SameEvn_idx=[]
    for p in range(0,len(Sim_Events)):
        SameEvn_idx.append(list(Tank_n[Tank_n.Event_no==p].index))
    
    f = len(SameEvn_idx)
    Tankx=[]
    results=[]
    for p in range(0,f):
        f=SameEvn_idx[p]
        Tankx=[]
        for q in range (0,len(f)):
            Tankx.append(Erftx_T[SameEvn_idx[p][q]])
            h=pd.concat(Tankx)
        results.append(h)
    
    return results    
        
Tank1_nEvents=ConcatEvents(Tank1_n,Sim_Events,Erft2_T1)
Tank2_nEvents=ConcatEvents(Tank2_n,Sim_Events,Erft3_T2)
Tank3_nEvents=ConcatEvents(Tank3_n,Sim_Events,Erft2_T3)
Tank4_nEvents=ConcatEvents(Tank4_n,Sim_Events,Erft3_T4)
Tank5_nEvents=ConcatEvents(Tank5_n,Sim_Events,Erft4_T5)
Tank6_nEvents=ConcatEvents(Tank6_n,Sim_Events,Erft2_T6)

def Overflow_Period(list_of_overflows):
    #Overflow volumes from each location, for each event
    Columns=['CSO10', 'CSO7', 'CSO8', 'CSO9', 'J1','J10', 'J11', 'J12', 'J13', 
             'J14', 'J15','J16', 'J17', 'J18', 'J19', 'J2', 'J3', 'J4', 
             'J5', 'J6', 'J7', 'J8', 'J9', 'Out_to_WWTP', 'T5', 'T4', 'T6', 
             'T3', 'T2', 'T1']
    list_ofOverflowOccurences=[]
    OVEnds=[]
    OVStarts=[]
    #For each dataframe in list
    for p in range(0,len(list_of_overflows)):
        df=list_of_overflows[p]
        df.columns=Columns
        EvNo=p
        #For each column in dataframe:
        for q in range(0,len(df.columns)):
            a=df.iloc[:,q]
            #Find start and end overflow even5
            b = list(map(int,[i != 0 for i in a]))
            idx = []   
            ind = 0    
            for n,i in enumerate(b):
                if (n+1 == len(b)) or (b[n] != b[n+1]):
                    idx.append((ind,n))
                    ind = n+1        
            if a[0]==0:
                idx=idx[1::2]        
            else: idx=idx[::2]
    
            if len(idx)>0:                       
                EventStart, EventEnd = zip(*idx)
4
==PAGE:115==
            else: 
                EventStart=[0]
                EventEnd=[0]
                
            OVEnds.append(EventEnd) 
            OVStarts.append(EventStart) 
            
            EventCumsum=[]
            EventDur_min=[]
            ColumnName=[]
            EvnNo=[]
            ColumnNo=[]
            for i in range(0,len(idx)):
                EventCumsum.append(a[EventStart[i]:EventEnd[i]+1].sum()*(60*5))
                EventDur_min.append(((EventEnd[i]+1)-EventStart[i])*5) 
                ColumnName.append(df.columns[q])
                EvnNo.append(EvNo)
                ColumnNo.append(q)
            
            G=pd.DataFrame({'EvnNo':EvnNo,'ColumnNo':ColumnNo,
                            'EventCumsum':EventCumsum,'EventDur_min':EventDur_min,
                            'ColumnName':ColumnName})
            list_ofOverflowOccurences.append(G)
    return list_ofOverflowOccurences, OVStarts, OVEnds

def Boxplot(boxPlot,Labels):
    # Boxplot of durations
    fig = plt.figure(1, figsize=(4, 3))
    ax = fig.add_subplot(111)
    bp = ax.boxplot(boxPlot, patch_artist=True)
    
    for box in bp['boxes']: # Boxes
        box.set( color='darkblue', linewidth=1)
    
    for whisker in bp['whiskers']: #Whiskers
        whisker.set(color='#7570b3', linewidth=1)
    
    for cap in bp['caps']: #Caps
        cap.set(color='#7570b3', linewidth=1)
    
    for median in bp['medians']: #Medians
18
==PAGE:129==
        median.set(color='red', linewidth=1)
    
    for flier in bp['fliers']: #fliers
        flier.set(marker='o', color='red', alpha=0.5)
    
    #Labels and titles        
    ax.set_xticklabels(Labels)
    plt.xlabel('Events')
    plt.ylabel('Precipitaiton [mm]')
    plt.tight_layout()
    plt.show()
    return plt.show()

                       MaxEvent_Erft_df], axis=1)

def CorrMatrix(Corr_matrix):
    
    fig, ax = plt.subplots(1,figsize=(8, 5))
    matrix = np.triu(Corr_matrix)
    labels=['P_sum [mm]','OV_sum [m3]','OV_sum/P_sum [%]','Event P [mm]',
            'Event D [min]','Event I [mm/hr]','5-min I [mm/hr]',
            '10-min I [mm/hr]','15-min I [mm/hr]','30-min I [mm/hr]']
    sb.heatmap(Corr_matrix, 
                xticklabels=labels,
                yticklabels=labels,
                cmap='RdBu_r',
                annot=True,
                mask=matrix,
                linewidth=0.5) 
            
    plt.tight_layout()
    return plt.show()
 
MaxCorr_matrix = Max_corr.corr(method="spearman")
MaxCorr_matrix_plt=CorrMatrix(MaxCorr_matrix)        
plt.savefig('Figures\\MaxCorr_matrix.png')
AvgCorr_matrix = Avg_corr.corr(method="spearman")
AvgCorr_matrix_plt=CorrMatrix(AvgCorr_matrix)
plt.savefig('Figures\\AvgCorr_matrix.png')
MaxCorr_matrix_overflows = (Max_corr.loc[Max_corr.Overflow_Vol>0]).corr(method="spearman")
MaxCorr_matrix_overflows_plt=CorrMatrix(MaxCorr_matrix_overflows)
plt.savefig('Figures\\MaxCorr_matrix_overflows.png')

Conf_upper=[]
mean1=[]
mean2=[]
std_1=[]
std_2=[]
N_1=[]
N_2=[]
df_f=[]
for x in range(min(Events.Year),max(Events.Year)+1):
    Year.append(x)
    data1 = Events.Overflow_Vol.loc[Events['Year'] != x] #all years - except sample year (independed)
    data2 = Events.Overflow_Vol.loc[Events['Year'] == x] #Sample of investegated year
    t_val, p_val = ttest_ind(data1, data2,equal_var = False) #t- and p-value
    t_value.append(t_val)
    p_value.append(p_val)
    print('t_val=%.3f, p_val=%.3f' % (t_val, p_val))

==PAGE:132==
    std1 = data1.std() #Standard deviations
    std2 = data2.std()
    N1 = len(data1) #Number of observations
    N2 = len(data2)
    
    mean1.append(data1.mean())
    mean2.append(data2.mean())
    std_1.append(std1)
    std_2.append(std2)
    N_1.append(N1)
    N_2.append(N2)
    
#    df = (len(data1) + len(data2) - 2) #Degree of freedom
    df = (data1.var()/N1 + data2.var()/N2)**2 / ((data1.var()/N1)**2 / (N1-1) + (data2.var()/N2)**2 / (N2-1))
    df_f.append(df)
    diff_mean = data1.mean()-data2.mean() #Difference between sample mean
    Diff_mean.append(diff_mean)
    SE_W = sqrt((std1**2/N1)+(std2**2/N2)) #Standard deviation of sample error
    conf = t.ppf(0.975, round(df)) * SE_W #for alpha = 5%
    Conf_up = diff_mean + conf #Upper limit of confidence interval
    Conf_upper.append(Conf_up)
    Conf_low = diff_mean - conf #Lower limit of confidence interval
    Conf_lower.append(Conf_low)
    
    if abs(t_val) < t.ppf(0.975, round(df)):
    
print('Probably the same distribution') #H0: the means of the samples are equal.
    else:
    
print('Probably different distributions')#H1: the means of the samples are unequal.
            
    if p_val > 0.05:
    
print('Probably the same distribution') #H0: the means of the samples are equal.
    else:
    
print('Probably different distributions')#H1: the means of the samples are unequal.
        
    print('diff_mean=%.3f [%3.f : %3.f]' % (diff_mean, Conf_low,Conf_up))
    
ttest = pd.DataFrame({'Year':Year,'df':df_f, 'mean1':mean1,'mean2':mean2,'std1':std_1, 
                      'std2':std_2, 'N1':N_1, 'N2':N_2, 't_value':t_value,
                      'p_value':p_value,'Diff_mean':Diff_mean, 
                      'Conf_lower':Conf_lower,'Conf_upper':Conf_upper})

def objective_func(K):
    simulation(K)  
    K=sum(K) #Add if optimiser used is NOT scalar
    #Import out-files for all events simulated using K-value
    files = glob.glob(r'C:\Users\Julie\OneDrive\Dokumenter\DTU\Master\MasterThesis\PySWMM\FW__Astelingen_model\Astlingen0
    files=Tcl().call('lsort', '-dict', files) #Sort based on name

    #If all events(years) should be included, using range(0,len(files))
    for x in range(0,len(files)): #Year 2003 is event no. 196-258
3
==PAGE:138==
        outfile=files[x]
        #What to import, use swmmtoolbox.listvariables(outfile) to see available data
        dff=swmmtoolbox.extract(outfile,['node','','Flow_lost_flooding'])
        list_of_overflows.append(dff) #List of dataframes
    
    Flood_sum1=[]
    for p in range(0,len(list_of_overflows)):
        df=list_of_overflows[p]
        df=df.reset_index(drop=True)
        df = df.sum(axis=0)*(60*5)
        
        #Sum of overflows to Main River (MR)
        
        MR=df[['node_T5_Flow_lost_flooding','node_T4_Flow_lost_flooding',
            'node_T3_Flow_lost_flooding','node_T2_Flow_lost_flooding']].copy()
        MRSUM=(MR.sum(axis=0)*2000) 
        
        #Sum of overflows to Park Creek (PC)
        PC=df[['node_T6_Flow_lost_flooding']].copy()
        PCSUM=(PC.sum(axis=0)*5000) 
        #Sum of overflows nearl WWTP (T1) (NWWTP)
        NWWTP=df[['node_T1_Flow_lost_flooding']]
        NWWTPSUM=(NWWTP.sum(axis=0)*1000) 
        
        #Sum of flood of city
        C=df.iloc[4:23]
        CSUM=C.sum(axis=0)*6000
        
        SUMTOT = [float(MRSUM),float(PCSUM),float(NWWTPSUM),float(CSUM)]
        SUMTOT = sum(SUMTOT)
        if SUMTOT>0:
            SUMTOT=np.log10(SUMTOT)
        
        Flood_sum1.append(SUMTOT)
    Flood_sum = sum(Flood_sum1)
    
    return Flood_sum

def objective_funcEVENTS(K):
    simulation(K)  
    K=sum(K) #Add if optimiser used is NOT scalar
    #Import out-files for all events simulated using K-value
    files = glob.glob(r'F:\Data\s144368\PySWMM\FW__Astelingen_model\Astlingen0920_RTCObj_WEvn_K%sEvn*.out'%K)
    files=Tcl().call('lsort', '-dict', files) #Sort based on name
    
    list_of_overflows=[]
    #If all events(years) should be included, using range(0,len(files))
    for x in range(0,len(files)): #Year 2003 is event no. 196-258
        outfile=files[x]
        #What to import, use swmmtoolbox.listvariables(outfile) to see available data
        dff=swmmtoolbox.extract(outfile,['node','','Flow_lost_flooding'])
        list_of_overflows.append(dff) #List of dataframes
     
    Flood_sum1=[]
    EventTot=[]
    
    for p in range(0,len(list_of_overflows)):
        df=list_of_overflows[p]
        df=df.reset_index(drop=True)
        df1 = df.sum(axis=1)
        df = df.sum(axis=0)*(60*5)
        
        #Sum of overflows to Main River (MR)
        
        MR=df[['node_T5_Flow_lost_flooding','node_T4_Flow_lost_flooding',
            'node_T3_Flow_lost_flooding','node_T2_Flow_lost_flooding']].copy()
        MRSUM=(MR.sum(axis=0)*2000) 
        
        #Sum of overflows to Park Creek (PC)
        PC=df[['node_T6_Flow_lost_flooding']].copy()
        PCSUM=(PC.sum(axis=0)*5000) 
        #Sum of overflows nearl WWTP (T1) (NWWTP)
        NWWTP=df[['node_T1_Flow_lost_flooding']]
        NWWTPSUM=(NWWTP.sum(axis=0)*1000) 
        
        #Sum of flood of city
        C=df.iloc[4:23]
5
==PAGE:140==
        CSUM=C.sum(axis=0)*6000
        
        SUMTOT = [float(MRSUM),float(PCSUM),float(NWWTPSUM),float(CSUM)]
        SUMTOT = sum(SUMTOT)
        if SUMTOT>0:
            SUMTOT=np.log10(SUMTOT)
            
                #Convert overflow volumes into df
        DF_overflow = pd.DataFrame(df1)
        #Changing the values in the df to zero and ones
        nonzero = np.sign(DF_overflow)
        for i in range(nonzero.shape[1]): 
            # .shape[1] = the number of columns
            # np.equal(df.iloc[:, i].values, 0) = gives True if two consecutive number are the same 
            #  np.concatenate(...) = join sequence and adds 0 at the start and end of the array 
            iszero = np.concatenate(([0], np.equal(nonzero.iloc[:, i].values, 0).view(np.int8), [0]))
            
            absdiff = np.abs(np.diff(iszero))
            
            # find where the zeros are located in the dataframe
            zerorange = np.where(absdiff == 1)[0].reshape(-1, 2)
            
            # for loop - writes 1 if the number of consecutive zeros are less than 120 
            # timesteps (dt). 1 dt is 0.5 seconds 
            for j in range(len(zerorange)):
                if zerorange[j][1] - zerorange[j][0] < 72:
                    nonzero.iloc[zerorange[j][0]:zerorange[j][1], i] = 1
        
        #Find non-zeros in dataframe
        nonzero = (nonzero != 0)
        #Assign index until zeros are found 
        group_ids = (nonzero & (nonzero != nonzero.shift())).cumsum()
        #Find maximum index corresponding to number of events
        Events=max(group_ids.iloc[:,0])
        EventTot.append(Events)
        SUMTOT2=SUMTOT*Events
        Flood_sum1.append(SUMTOT2)
    Flood_sum = sum(Flood_sum1)
        
    return Flood_sum

E. The Equal Filling Degree control strategy 
The rules applied in the provided model containing the rules for Equal Filling Degree. The rules are copied 
pasted into the control editor of a SWMM model of the ‘Astlingen’ benchmark model.   
RULE EFDO1 
IF  NODE T2 DEPTH < 1 
AND NODE T3 DEPTH < 1 
AND NODE T4 DEPTH < 1 
AND NODE T6 DEPTH < 1 
THEN ORIFICE V2 SETTING = 0.2366 
AND  ORIFICE V3 SETTING = 0.6508 
AND  ORIFICE V4 SETTING = 0.3523 
AND  ORIFICE V6 SETTING = 0.4303

APPENDIX 
F. Nominal or maximum throttle flow 
A small comparison of the impacts of applying a nominal flow or maximum flow. The Base Case with 
maximum throttle and the SC model using the nominal flow is presented for for Tank 6.   
An initial screening of the system, including investigating the filling of the downstream system (pipes and 
storage unit Tank 3) when Tank 6 overflows, reviles that storage unit Tank 6 overflows due to the nominal 
throttle flow, resulting in CSOs from the storage unit, even when the downstream pipes and storage unit Tank 
3 has capacity to receive water, but the water never enters the downstream pipes.  
 
Figure 45: Elevation profile from storage unit T6 to storage unit T3 at 2003-01-12 09:45. The elevation profile is made in SWMM. 
During the simulation period 2003-01-12 09:45 to 2003-01, storage unit T6 overflows despite of available 
capacity downstream in the system, in pipes as well as the storage unit T3. There is no storage unit upstream 
of T6, hence no water can be held back to reduce the CSOs of the storage unit. Thereby RTC of the initial 
system is not considered feasible.  
The results and changes of increasing the throttle flow from Tank 6 from the nominal flow (the provided model 
SC) 76 L/s to 175 L/s (the maximum flow used in the Base Case) is presented below.  
Table 27: The results of applying the maximum flow from Tank 6 (175 L/s). The changes from the nominal flow (76 L/s) are shown 
as percentage.

Increasing the throttle flow from Tank 6 impacts the number of CSO events and volumes from storage unit 
Tank 1 and Tank 3, both located downstream of Tank 6. The decrease in CSO events from Tank 6 results in a 
reduction of 75% of the CSO volumes. Storage unit Tank 1 is located directly upstream of the WWTP, hence 
receives water from all storage units, hence an increase in the CSO upstream is expected to impact the storage 
unit. The impact results in an additional CSO event occurring from Tank 1, and an increase of 4% of the total 
==PAGE:146==
APPENDIX to Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating 
on selecting events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 87 of 88 
APPENDIX 
CSO volume. For storage unit Tank 3, the change results in an increase of eleven CSO events occurring from 
eleven additional simulation periods. The total CSO volume from Tank 3 is more than doubled. Despite of the 
increase in CSO events, the decrease of Tank 6 results in the total simulations periods where CSOs occurs is 
decreased from 37 simulation periods to 33, and a minor reduction of the total CSO volume (2% less).   
To ensure the increase of the throttle flow from storage unit Tank 6 leads to an increase in the RTC potential 
and not just results in the problem being moved further downstream to T3, the state of the system surrounding 
Tank 3 is investigated. A simulation periods causing CSOs at storage unit T3 is random selected, the elevation 
profile from two of the storage unit from which capacity can be utilized to increase the RTC potential and 
reduce CSOs from Tank 3 is presented in Figure 46.

APPENDIX to Computationally efficient estimation of real time control parameters in SWMM, concentrating 
on selecting events from long-term historical rain series 
Page 88 of 88 
APPENDIX 
From the elevation profiles presented in Figure 46 it can be seen that, capacity to store water in the upstream 
storage units (Tank 6 and Tank 4) is available during the CSOs from Tank 3, hence there is potential of 
reducing CSOs from Tank 3 using RTC. During this event, storage unit Tank 3 and Tank 1 overflows, along 
with two of the four CSO structures. There is some available capacity at T5, hence this could be utilized as 
well.
